Staffordshire">Staffordshire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Suffolk" title="History of Suffolk">Suffolk</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Surrey" class="mw-redirect" title="History of Yorkshire">West Yorkshire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Wiltshire" title="History of Wiltshire">Wiltshire</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Worcestershire" title="History of Worcestershire">Worcestershire</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0 0.1em 0.4em">\n<div class="NavFrame collapsed" style="border:none;padding:0"><div class="NavHead" town</a></div><div class="NavContent" style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center"><div class="hlist">\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/History_of_Birmingham" title="History of York">York</a></li></ul>\n</div></div></div></td>\n</tr><tr><td style="padding:0.3em 0.4em 0.3em;font-weight:bold;border-top: 1px solid #aaa; border-bottom: 1px solid #aaa;">\n<a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_England.svg" class="image"><img alt="Flag of England.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/16px-Flag_of_England.svg.png" width="16" height="10" class="noviewer" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/24px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/be/Flag_of_England.svg/32px-Flag_of_England.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="480" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Portal:England" title="Portal:England">England&#32;portal</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:History_of_England" title="Template:History of England"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:History_of_England" title="Template talk:History of England"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:History_of_England&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>England in the Middle Ages</b> concerns the <a href="/wiki/History_of_England" title="History of England">history of England</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">medieval period</a>, from the end of the 5th century through to the start of the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_Britain" title="Early modern Britain">Early Modern period</a> in 1485. When England emerged from the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of England in the Middle Ages">economy</a> was in tatters and many of the towns abandoned. After several centuries of Germanic immigration, new identities and cultures began to emerge, developing into kingdoms that competed for power. A rich <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_art" title="Anglo-Saxon art">artistic culture</a> flourished under the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxons" title="Anglo-Saxons">Anglo-Saxons</a>, producing <a href="/wiki/Epic_poetry" title="Epic poetry">epic poems</a> such as <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i> and sophisticated <a href="/wiki/Metalwork" class="mw-redirect" title="Metalwork">metalwork</a>. The Anglo-Saxons converted to Christianity in the 7th century and a network of <a href="/wiki/Monastery" title="Monastery">monasteries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Convent" title="Convent">convents</a> was built across England. In the 8th and 9th centuries England faced fierce <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> attacks, and the fighting lasted for many decades, eventually establishing <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a> as the most powerful kingdom and promoting the growth of an English identity. Despite repeated crises of succession and a <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danish</a> seizure of power at the start of the 11th century, by the 1060s England was a powerful, centralised state with a strong military and successful economy.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman invasion of England">Norman invasion of England</a> in 1066 led to the defeat and replacement of the Anglo-Saxon elite with Norman and French nobles and their supporters. <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William the Conqueror</a> and his successors took over the existing state system, repressing local revolts and controlling the population through <a href="/wiki/Castles_in_Great_Britain_and_Ireland#Norman_Invasion" title="Castles in Great Britain and Ireland">a network of castles</a>. The new rulers introduced a <a href="/wiki/Feudalism_in_England" title="Feudalism in England">feudal approach</a> to governing England, eradicating the practice of <a href="/wiki/Slavery" title="Slavery">slavery</a> but creating a much wider body of unfree labourers called <a href="/wiki/Serf" class="mw-redirect" title="Serf">serfs</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Women in the Middle Ages">position of women in society</a> changed as laws regarding land and lordship shifted. <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_England" title="Demography of England">England\'s population</a> more than doubled during the 12th and 13th centuries, fuelling an expansion of the towns, cities and trade, helped by <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">warmer temperatures</a> across Northern Europe. A new wave of monasteries and friaries were established, while <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Reform" title="Gregorian Reform">ecclesiastical reforms</a> led to tensions between successive kings and <a href="/wiki/Archbishop" title="Archbishop">archbishops</a>. Despite developments in England\'s governance and <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">legal system</a>, infighting between the Anglo-Norman elite resulted in multiple civil wars and the <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England#Loss_of_Normandy,_1202\xe2\x80\x9304" title="John, King of England">loss of Normandy</a>. \n</p><p>The 14th century in England saw the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%931317" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Famine of 1315\xe2\x80\x931317">Great Famine</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a>, catastrophic events that killed around half of England\'s population, throwing the economy into chaos and undermining the old political order. Social unrest followed, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants&#39; Revolt">Peasants\' Revolt</a> of 1381, while the changes in the economy resulted in the emergence of a new class of <a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">gentry</a>, and the nobility began to exercise power through a system termed <a href="/wiki/Bastard_feudalism" title="Bastard feudalism">bastard feudalism</a>. Nearly 1,500 <a href="/wiki/Deserted_medieval_village" class="mw-redirect" title="Deserted medieval village">villages were deserted</a> by their inhabitants and many men and women sought new opportunities in the towns and cities. New technologies were introduced, and England produced some of the great <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">medieval philosophers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Natural_science" title="Natural science">natural scientists</a>. English kings in the 14th and 15th centuries laid claim to the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_France" title="Kingdom of France">French throne</a>, resulting in the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a>. At times England enjoyed huge military success, with the economy buoyed by profits from the international wool and cloth trade, but by 1450 the country was in crisis, facing military failure in France and an ongoing recession. More social unrest broke out, followed by the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>, fought between rival factions of the English nobility. <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>\'s victory in 1485 conventionally marks the end of the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> in England and the start of the <a href="/wiki/Early_Modern_period" class="mw-redirect" title="Early Modern period">Early Modern period</a>.\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-4"><div id="toc" class="toctext">Surveys</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-37"><a href="#Kings"><span class="tocnumber">10.2</span> <span class="toctext">Kings</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 class="toclevel-2 tocsection-39"><a href="#Specialized_studies"><span class="tocnumber">10.4</span> <span class="toctext">Specialized studies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-40"><a href="#Historiography_2"><span class="tocnumber">10.5</span> <span class="toctext">Historiography</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_history">Political history</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Political history">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span id="Early_Middle_Ages_.28600.E2.80.931066.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages_(600\xe2\x80\x931066)">Early Middle Ages (600\xe2\x80\x931066)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Early Middle Ages (600\xe2\x80\x931066)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Anglo-Saxon_England" title="History of Anglo-Saxon England">History of Anglo-Saxon England</a></div>\n<p>At the start of the Middle Ages, England was a part of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Britain" title="Roman Britain">Britannia</a>, a former province of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. The <a href="/wiki/English_Economy" class="mw-redirect" title="English Economy">local economy</a> had once been dominated by imperial Roman spending on a large military establishment, which in turn helped to support a complex network of towns, roads, and <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">villas</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> At the end of the 4th century, however, Roman forces had been largely withdrawn, and this economy collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> Germanic immigrants began to arrive in increasing numbers during the 5th century, establishing small farms and settlements,<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup> and their language, <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, <a href="/wiki/Celtic_language-death_in_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic language-death in England">swiftly spread</a> as people switched from <a href="/wiki/British_Celtic" class="mw-redirect" title="British Celtic">British Celtic</a> and <a href="/wiki/British_Latin" title="British Latin">British Latin</a> to the language of this new elite.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> New political and social identities emerged, including an <a href="/wiki/Angles" title="Angles">Anglian</a> culture in the east of England and a <a href="/wiki/Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Saxon">Saxon</a> culture in the south, with local groups establishing <i>regiones</i>, small polities ruled over by powerful families and individuals.<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> By the 7th century, some rulers, including those of <a href="/wiki/Wessex" title="Wessex">Wessex</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_East_Anglia" title="Kingdom of East Anglia">East Anglia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Essex" title="Kingdom of Essex">Essex</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Kent" title="Kingdom of Kent">Kent</a>, had begun to term themselves kings, living in <i>villae regales</i>, royal centres, and collecting tribute from the surrounding <i>regiones</i>; these kingdoms are often referred to as <a href="/wiki/The_Heptarchy" class="mw-redirect" title="The Heptarchy">the Heptarchy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png/220px-Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png" width="220" height="315" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png/330px-Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ee/Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png/440px-Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png 2x" data-file-width="1949" data-file-height="2791" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton_Hoo_helmet_2016.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ceremonial <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon">Anglo-Saxon</a> helmet from the <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Hoo" title="Sutton Hoo">Sutton Hoo</a> burial, 7th century</div></div></div>\n<p>In the 7th century, the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Mercia" class="mw-redirect" title="Kingdom of Mercia">kingdom of Mercia</a> rose to prominence under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Penda_of_Mercia" title="Penda of Mercia">King Penda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Fleming,_p._205_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming,_p._205-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> Mercia invaded neighbouring lands until it loosely controlled around 50 <i>regiones</i> covering much of England.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Mercia and the remaining kingdoms, led by their warrior elites, continued to compete for territory throughout the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> Massive earthworks, such as the <a href="/wiki/Offa%27s_Dyke" title="Offa&#39;s Dyke">defensive dyke</a> built by <a href="/wiki/Offa_of_Mercia" title="Offa of Mercia">Offa of Mercia</a>, helped to defend key frontiers and towns.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> In 789, however, the first <a href="/wiki/Scandinavia" title="Scandinavia">Scandinavian</a> raids on England began; these <a href="/wiki/Viking" class="mw-redirect" title="Viking">Viking</a> attacks grew in number and scale until in 865 the <a href="/wiki/Danes_(Germanic_tribe)" title="Danes (Germanic tribe)">Danish</a> <i>micel here</i> or <a href="/wiki/Great_Heathen_Army" title="Great Heathen Army">Great Army</a>, invaded England, captured <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a> and defeated the kingdom of East Anglia.<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Mercia and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Northumbria" title="Kingdom of Northumbria">Northumbria</a> fell in 875 and 876, and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_the_Great" title="Alfred the Great">Alfred of Wessex</a> was driven into internal exile in 878.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>However, in the same year Alfred won a decisive victory against the Danes at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Edington" title="Battle of Edington">Battle of Edington</a>, and he exploited the fear of the Viking threat to raise large numbers of men and using a network of defended towns called <i><a href="/wiki/Burh" title="Burh">burhs</a></i> to defend his territory and mobilise royal resources.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Suppressing internal opposition to his rule, Alfred contained the invaders within a region known as the <a href="/wiki/Danelaw" title="Danelaw">Danelaw</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Under his son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Elder" title="Edward the Elder">Edward the Elder</a>, and his grandson, <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelstan" title="\xc3\x86thelstan">\xc3\x86thelstan</a>, Wessex expanded further north into Mercia and the Danelaw, and by the 950s and the reigns of <a href="/wiki/Eadred" title="Eadred">Eadred</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edgar_the_Peaceful" title="Edgar the Peaceful">Edgar</a>, York was finally permanently retaken from the Vikings.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> The West Saxon rulers were now kings of the <i>Angelcynn</i>, that is of the whole English folk.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>With the death of Edgar, however, the royal succession became problematic.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_the_Unready" title="\xc3\x86thelred the Unready">\xc3\x86thelred</a> took power in 978 following the murder of his brother <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Martyr" title="Edward the Martyr">Edward</a>, but England was then invaded by <a href="/wiki/Sweyn_Forkbeard" title="Sweyn Forkbeard">Sweyn Forkbeard</a>, the son of a Danish king.<sup id="cite_ref-Fleming,_pp._314-315_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming,_pp._314-315-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Attempts to bribe Sweyn not to attack using <a href="/wiki/Danegeld" title="Danegeld">danegeld</a> payments failed, and he took the throne in 1013.<sup id="cite_ref-Fleming,_pp._314-315_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming,_pp._314-315-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> Swein\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Cnut_the_Great" title="Cnut the Great">Cnut</a>, liquidated many of the older English families following his seizure of power in 1016.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingP315_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingP315-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> \xc3\x86thelred\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_the_Confessor" title="Edward the Confessor">Edward the Confessor</a>, had survived in exile in Normandy and returned to claim the throne in 1042.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingP315_19-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingP315-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> Edward was childless, and the succession again became a concern.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingP315_19-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingP315-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> England became dominated by the Godwin family, who had taken advantage of the Danish killings to acquire huge wealth. When Edward died in 1066, <a href="/wiki/Harold_Godwinson" title="Harold Godwinson">Harold Godwinson</a> claimed the throne, defeating his rival Norwegian claimant, <a href="/wiki/Harald_Hardrada" title="Harald Hardrada">Harald Hardrada</a>, at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Stamford_Bridge" title="Battle of Stamford Bridge">battle of Stamford Bridge</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="High_Middle_Ages_.281066.E2.80.931272.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Middle_Ages_(1066\xe2\x80\x931272)">High Middle Ages (1066\xe2\x80\x931272)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: High Middle Ages (1066\xe2\x80\x931272)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="England in the High Middle Ages">England in the High Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Norman England">Anglo-Norman England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg/220px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg" width="220" height="192" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg/330px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg/440px-Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2948" data-file-height="2568" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Bayeux_Tapestry_scene57_Harold_death.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Section of the Bayeux Tapestry showing the final stages of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">battle of Hastings</a></div></div></div>\n<p>In 1066, <a href="/wiki/William_the_Conqueror" title="William the Conqueror">William</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Duke_of_Normandy" title="Duke of Normandy">Duke of Normandy</a>, took advantage of the English succession crisis to <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest_of_England" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman Conquest of England">invade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup> With an army of <a href="/wiki/Normans" title="Normans">Norman followers</a> and mercenaries, he defeated Harold at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Hastings" title="Battle of Hastings">battle of Hastings</a> and rapidly occupied the south of England.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> William used a network of <a href="/wiki/Castle" title="Castle">castles</a> to control the major centres of power, granting extensive lands to his main Norman followers and co-opting or eliminating the former Anglo-Saxon elite.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> Major revolts followed, which William suppressed before intervening in the north-east of England, establishing Norman control of York and <a href="/wiki/Harrying_of_the_North" title="Harrying of the North">devastating the region</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Some Norman lords used England as a launching point for attacks into <a href="/wiki/South_Wales" title="South Wales">South</a> and <a href="/wiki/North_Wales" title="North Wales">North Wales</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norman_conquest_of_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman conquest of Wales">spreading up the valleys</a> to create new <a href="/wiki/Marcher_Lord" title="Marcher Lord">Marcher</a> territories.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> By the time of William\'s death in 1087, England formed the largest part of an Anglo-Norman empire, ruled over by a network of nobles with landholdings across England, Normandy, and Wales.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> England\'s growing wealth was critical in allowing the Norman kings to project power across the region, including funding campaigns along the frontiers of Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Norman rule, however, proved unstable; successions to the throne were contested, leading to violent conflicts between the claimants and their noble supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_II_of_England" title="William II of England">William II</a> inherited the throne but faced revolts attempting to replace him with his older brother <a href="/wiki/Robert_Curthose" title="Robert Curthose">Robert</a> or his cousin <a href="/wiki/Stephen_of_Aumale" title="Stephen of Aumale">Stephen of Aumale</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> In 1100, William II died while hunting. Despite Robert\'s rival claims, his younger brother <a href="/wiki/Henry_I_of_England" title="Henry I of England">Henry I</a> immediately seized power.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> War broke out, ending in Robert\'s defeat at <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tinchebray" title="Battle of Tinchebray">Tinchebrai</a> and his subsequent life imprisonment. Robert\'s son <a href="/wiki/William_Clito" title="William Clito">Clito</a> remained free, however, and formed the focus for fresh revolts until his death in 1128.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s only legitimate son, <a href="/wiki/William_Adelin" title="William Adelin">William</a>, died aboard the <i><a href="/wiki/White_Ship" title="White Ship">White Ship</a></i> disaster of 1120, sparking a fresh succession crisis: Henry\'s nephew, <a href="/wiki/Stephen,_King_of_England" title="Stephen, King of England">Stephen of Blois</a>, claimed the throne in 1135, but this was disputed by the <a href="/wiki/Empress_Matilda" title="Empress Matilda">Empress Matilda</a>, Henry\'s daughter.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup> Civil war broke out across England and Normandy, resulting in a long period of warfare later termed <a href="/wiki/The_Anarchy" title="The Anarchy">the Anarchy</a>. Matilda\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_II_of_England" title="Henry II of England">Henry</a>, finally agreed to a peace settlement at <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Winchester" class="mw-redirect" title="Treaty of Winchester">Winchester</a> and succeeded as king in 1154.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Henry II was the first of the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_kings_of_England" title="Angevin kings of England">Angevin</a> rulers of England, so-called because he was also the <a href="/wiki/Counts_and_dukes_of_Anjou" title="Counts and dukes of Anjou">Count of Anjou</a> in Northern France.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> Henry had also acquired the huge <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Aquitaine" title="Duchy of Aquitaine">duchy of Aquitaine</a> by marriage, and England became a key part of a loose-knit assemblage of lands spread across Western Europe, later termed the <a href="/wiki/Angevin_Empire" title="Angevin Empire">Angevin Empire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup> Henry reasserted royal authority and rebuilt the royal finances, <a href="/wiki/Norman_invasion_of_Ireland" title="Norman invasion of Ireland">intervening to claim power in Ireland</a> and promoting the Anglo-Norman colonisation of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Henry strengthened England\'s borders with Wales and Scotland, and used the country\'s wealth to fund a long-running war with his rivals in France, but arrangements for his succession once again proved problematic.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> Several revolts broke out, led by Henry\'s children who were eager to acquire power and lands, sometimes backed by France, Scotland and the Welsh princes. After a final confrontation with Henry, his son <a href="/wiki/Richard_I_of_England" title="Richard I of England">Richard I</a> succeeded to the throne in 1189.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Richard spent his reign focused on protecting his possessions in France and fighting in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third Crusade</a>; his brother, <a href="/wiki/John,_King_of_England" title="John, King of England">John</a>, inherited England in 1199 but lost Normandy and most of Aquitaine after several years of war with France.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> John fought successive, increasingly expensive, campaigns in a bid to regain these possessions.<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> John\'s efforts to raise revenues, combined with his fractious relationships with many of the English barons, led to confrontation in 1215, an attempt to restore peace through the signing of the <i><a href="/wiki/Magna_Carta" title="Magna Carta">Magna Carta</a></i>, and finally the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/First_Barons%27_War" title="First Barons&#39; War">First Barons\' War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> John died having fought the rebel barons and their French backers to a stalemate, and royal power was re-established by barons loyal to the young <a href="/wiki/Henry_III_of_England" title="Henry III of England">Henry III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup> England\'s power structures remained unstable and the outbreak of the <a href="/wiki/Second_Barons%27_War" title="Second Barons&#39; War">Second Barons\' War</a> in 1264 resulted in the king\'s capture by <a href="/wiki/Simon_de_Montfort,_6th_Earl_of_Leicester" title="Simon de Montfort, 6th Earl of Leicester">Simon de Montfort</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup> Henry\'s son, <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England" title="Edward I of England">Edward</a>, defeated the rebel factions between 1265 and 1267, restoring his father to power.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Late_Middle_Ages_.281272.E2.80.931485.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Middle_Ages_(1272\xe2\x80\x931485)">Late Middle Ages (1272\xe2\x80\x931485)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Late Middle Ages (1272\xe2\x80\x931485)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_Late_Middle_Ages" title="England in the Late Middle Ages">England in the Late Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean_Froissart,_Chroniques,_154v,_12148_btv1b8438605hf336,_crop.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg/220px-Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg" width="220" height="212" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg/330px-Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg/440px-Jean_Froissart%2C_Chroniques%2C_154v%2C_12148_btv1b8438605hf336%2C_crop.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3261" data-file-height="3144" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Jean_Froissart,_Chroniques,_154v,_12148_btv1b8438605hf336,_crop.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard&#160;II</a> meets the rebels calling for economic and political reform during the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants&#39; Revolt">Peasants\' Revolt</a> of 1381</div></div></div>\n<p>On becoming king, Edward I rebuilt the status of the monarchy, restoring and extending key castles that had fallen into disrepair.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup> Uprisings by the princes of North Wales led to Edward mobilising a huge army, <a href="/wiki/Edward_I_of_England#Welsh_wars" title="Edward I of England">defeating the native Welsh</a> and undertaking a programme of English colonisation and castle building across the region.<sup id="cite_ref-48" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup> Further wars were conducted in <a href="/wiki/Flanders" title="Flanders">Flanders</a> and Aquitaine.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Edward also fought <a href="/wiki/First_War_of_Scottish_Independence" title="First War of Scottish Independence">campaigns in Scotland</a>, but was unable to achieve strategic victory, and the costs created tensions that nearly led to civil war.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_II_of_England" title="Edward II of England">Edward II</a> inherited the war with Scotland and faced growing opposition to his rule as a result of his royal favourites and military failures.<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Despenser_War" title="Despenser War">Despenser War</a> of 1321\xe2\x80\x9322 was followed by instability and the subsequent overthrow, and possible murder, of Edward in 1327 at the hands of his French wife, <a href="/wiki/Isabella_of_France" title="Isabella of France">Isabella</a>, and a rebel baron, <a href="/wiki/Roger_Mortimer,_1st_Earl_of_March" title="Roger Mortimer, 1st Earl of March">Roger Mortimer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup> Isabella and Mortimer\'s regime lasted only a few years before falling to a coup, led by Isabella\'s son <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, in 1330.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Like his grandfather, Edward III took steps to restore royal power, but during the 1340s the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> arrived in England.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> The losses from the epidemic, and the recurring plagues that followed it, significantly affected events in England for many years to come.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> Meanwhile, Edward, under pressure from France in Aquitaine, made a challenge for the French throne.<sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup> Over the next century, English forces fought many campaigns in a long-running conflict that became known as the <a href="/wiki/Hundred_Years%27_War" title="Hundred Years&#39; War">Hundred Years\' War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> Despite the challenges involved in raising the revenues to pay for the war, Edward\'s military successes brought an influx of plundered wealth to many parts of England and enabled substantial building work by the king.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup> Many members of the English elite, including Edward\'s son <a href="/wiki/Edward,_the_Black_Prince" class="mw-redirect" title="Edward, the Black Prince">the Black Prince</a>, were heavily involved in campaigning in France and administering the new continental territories.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Edward\'s grandson, the young <a href="/wiki/Richard_II_of_England" title="Richard II of England">Richard II</a>, faced political and economic problems, many resulting from the Black Death, including the <a href="/wiki/Peasants%27_Revolt" title="Peasants&#39; Revolt">Peasants\' Revolt</a> that broke out across the south of England in 1381.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup> Over the coming decades, Richard and groups of nobles vied for power and control of policy towards France until <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV_of_England" title="Henry IV of England">Henry of Bolingbroke</a> seized the throne with the support of <a href="/wiki/Parliament_of_England" title="Parliament of England">parliament</a> in 1399.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Ruling as Henry IV, he exercised power through a royal council and parliament, while attempting to enforce political and religious conformity.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup> His son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_V_of_England" title="Henry V of England">Henry V</a>, reinvigorated the war with France and came close to achieving strategic success shortly before his death in 1422.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI_of_England" title="Henry VI of England">Henry VI</a> became king at the age of only nine months and both the English political system and the military situation in France began to unravel.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>A sequence of bloody civil wars, later termed the <a href="/wiki/Wars_of_the_Roses" title="Wars of the Roses">Wars of the Roses</a>, finally broke out in 1455, spurred on by an  economic crisis and a widespread perception of poor government.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_IV_of_England" title="Edward IV of England">Edward IV</a>, leading a faction known as the <a href="/wiki/House_of_York" title="House of York">Yorkists</a>, removed Henry from power in 1461 but by 1469 fighting recommenced as Edward, Henry, and Edward\'s brother <a href="/wiki/George_Plantagenet,_1st_Duke_of_Clarence" title="George Plantagenet, 1st Duke of Clarence">George</a>, backed by leading nobles and powerful French supporters, vied for power.<sup id="cite_ref-HicksP5_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HicksP5-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> By 1471 Edward was triumphant and most of his rivals were dead.<sup id="cite_ref-HicksP5_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HicksP5-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> On his death, power passed to his brother <a href="/wiki/Richard_III_of_England" title="Richard III of England">Richard of Gloucester</a>, who initially ruled on behalf of the young <a href="/wiki/Edward_V_of_England" title="Edward V of England">Edward V</a> before seizing the throne himself as Richard III.<sup id="cite_ref-HicksP5_68-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HicksP5-68">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> The future <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII_of_England" title="Henry VII of England">Henry VII</a>, aided by French and Scottish troops, returned to England and defeated Richard at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Bosworth" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Bosworth">battle of Bosworth</a> in 1485, bringing an end to the majority of the fighting, although lesser rebellions against his <a href="/wiki/Tudor_dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Tudor dynasty">Tudor dynasty</a> would continue for several years afterwards.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Government_and_society">Government and society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Government and society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Governance_and_social_structures">Governance and social structures</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Governance and social structures">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<h4><span id="Early_Middle_Ages_.28600.E2.80.931066.29_2"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages_(600\xe2\x80\x931066)_2">Early Middle Ages (600\xe2\x80\x931066)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Early Middle Ages (600\xe2\x80\x931066)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_England#Early_medieval_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Social history of England">Social history of the Early Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg/220px-Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg" width="220" height="223" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg/330px-Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg/440px-Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="689" data-file-height="697" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Aethelred_II_gold_mancus_1003_1006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An Anglo-Saxon <a href="/wiki/Mancus" title="Mancus">mancus</a>, showing the face of <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelred_the_Unready" title="\xc3\x86thelred the Unready">\xc3\x86thelred the Unready</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Anglo-Saxon kingdoms were <a href="/wiki/Social_stratification" title="Social stratification">hierarchical</a> societies, each based on ties of allegiance between powerful lords and their immediate followers.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> At the top of the social structure was the king, who stood above many of the normal processes of Anglo-Saxon life and whose household had special privileges and protection.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup> Beneath the king were <i><a href="/wiki/Thegns" class="mw-redirect" title="Thegns">thegns</a></i>, nobles, the more powerful of which maintained their own courts and were termed <i><a href="/wiki/Ealdorman" title="Ealdorman">ealdormen</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup> The relationship between kings and their nobles was bound up with military symbolism and the ritual exchange of weapons and armour.<sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup> Freemen, called <i><a href="/wiki/Churls" class="mw-redirect" title="Churls">churls</a></i>, formed the next level of society, often holding land in their own right or controlling businesses in the towns.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_pp._97-99_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_pp._97-99-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> <i>Geburs</i>, peasants who worked land belonging to a <i>thegn</i>, formed a lower class still.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The very lowest class were slaves, who could be bought and sold and who held only minimal rights.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The balance of power between these different groups changed over time. Early in the period, kings were elected by members of the late king\'s council, but <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a> rapidly became the norm for succession.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> The kings further bolstered their status by adopting Christian ceremonies and nomenclature, introducing ecclesiastical coronations during the 8th century and terming themselves "Christ\'s deputy" by the 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup> Huge estates were initially built up by the king, bishops, monasteries and <i>thegns</i>, but in the 9th and 10th centuries these were slowly broken up as a consequence of inheritance arrangements, marriage settlements and church purchases.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup> In the 11th century, the royal position worsened further, as the <i>ealdormen</i> rapidly built up huge new estates, making them collectively much more powerful than the king\xe2\x80\x94this contributed to the political instability of the final Anglo-Saxon years.<sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup> As time went by, the position of the <i>churls</i> deteriorated, as their rights were slowly eroded and their duties to their lords increased.<sup id="cite_ref-Whitelock,_pp._97-99_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Whitelock,_pp._97-99-74">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The kingdom of Wessex, which eventually laid claim to England as a whole, evolved a centralised royal administration. One part of this was the king\'s council, the <i><a href="/wiki/Witenagemot" title="Witenagemot">witenagemot</a></i>, comprising the senior clergy, <i>ealdormen</i>, and some of the more important <i>thegns</i>; the council met to advise the king on policy and legal issues.<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;79&#93;</a></sup> The royal household included officials, <i>thegns</i> and a <a href="/wiki/Secretariat_(administrative_office)" title="Secretariat (administrative office)">secretariat</a> of clergy which travelled with the king, conducting the affairs of government as it went.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> Under the Danish kings, a bodyguard of <a href="/wiki/Housecarl" title="Housecarl">housecarls</a> also accompanied the court.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> At a regional level, <i>ealdormen</i> played an important part in government, defence and taxation, and the post of <a href="/wiki/Sheriff" title="Sheriff">sheriff</a> emerged in the 10th century, administering local <a href="/wiki/Shire" title="Shire">shires</a> on behalf of an ealdorman.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup> Anglo-Saxon <a href="/wiki/Mint_(coin)" class="mw-redirect" title="Mint (coin)">mints</a> were tightly controlled by the kings, providing a high-quality currency, and the whole country was taxed using a system called <a href="/wiki/Hidage" class="mw-redirect" title="Hidage">hidage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Anglo-Saxon kings built up a set of written laws, issued either as statutes or codes, but these laws were never written down in their entirety and were always supplemented by an extensive oral tradition of customary law.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup> In the early part of the period local assemblies called <a href="/wiki/Folkmoot" class="mw-redirect" title="Folkmoot">moots</a> were gathered to apply the laws to particular cases; in the 10th century these were replaced by <a href="/wiki/Hundred_court" class="mw-redirect" title="Hundred court">hundred courts</a>, serving local areas, and <a href="/wiki/Shire_moot" class="mw-redirect" title="Shire moot">shire moots</a> dealing with larger regions of the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> Many churchmen and <i>thegns</i> were also given permission by the king to hold their own local courts.<sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup> The legal system depended on a system of <a href="/wiki/Oath" title="Oath">oaths</a>, in which the value of different individuals swearing on behalf of the plaintiff or defendant varied according to their social status - the word of a companion of the king, for example, was worth twelve times that of a <i>churl</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-89" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> If fines were imposed, their size similarly varied accord to the oath-value of the individual.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> The Anglo-Saxon authorities struggled to deal with the <a href="/wiki/Bloodfeud" class="mw-redirect" title="Bloodfeud">bloodfeuds</a> between families that emerged following violent killings, attempting to use a system of <i><a href="/wiki/Weregild" title="Weregild">weregild</a></i>, a payment of blood money, as a way of providing an alternative to long-running vendettas.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="High_Middle_Ages_.281066.E2.80.931272.29_2"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Middle_Ages_(1066\xe2\x80\x931272)_2">High Middle Ages (1066\xe2\x80\x931272)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: High Middle Ages (1066\xe2\x80\x931272)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/England_in_the_High_Middle_Ages" title="England in the High Middle Ages">England in the High Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_England#Late_medieval_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Social history of England">Social history of the High Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg/220px-Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg" width="220" height="224" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg/330px-Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg/440px-Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1153" data-file-height="1176" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Anglo-Norman_-_Game_Piece_with_Enthroned_Figure_-_Walters_71141.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anglo-Norman 12th-century <a href="/wiki/Board_game" title="Board game">gaming piece</a>, illustrating soldiers presenting a sheep to a figure seated on a throne</div></div></div>\n<p>Within twenty years of the Norman conquest, the former Anglo-Saxon elite were replaced by a new class of Norman nobility, with around 8,000 Normans and French settling in England.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> The new <a href="/wiki/Earl" title="Earl">earls</a> (successors to the ealdermen), sheriffs and church seniors were all drawn from their ranks.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup> In many areas of society there was continuity, as the Normans adopted many of the Anglo-Saxon governmental institutions, including the tax system, mints and the centralisation of law-making and some judicial matters; initially sheriffs and the hundred courts continued to function as before.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The existing tax liabilities were captured in <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i>, produced in 1086.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Changes in other areas soon began to be felt. The method of government after the conquest can be described as a <a href="/wiki/Feudalism_in_England" title="Feudalism in England">feudal system</a>, in that the new nobles held their lands on behalf of the king; in return for promising to provide military support and taking an oath of allegiance, called <a href="/wiki/Homage_(feudal)" title="Homage (feudal)">homage</a>, they were granted lands termed a <a href="/wiki/Fief" title="Fief">fief</a> or an <a href="/wiki/Honour_(feudal_land_tenure)" class="mw-redirect" title="Honour (feudal land tenure)">honour</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">&#91;nb 3&#93;</a></sup> Major nobles in turn granted lands to smaller landowners in return for homage and further military support, and eventually the peasantry held land in return for local labour services, creating a web of loyalties and resources enforced in part by new honorial courts.<sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup> This system had been used in Normandy and concentrated more power in the king and the upper elite than the former Anglo-Saxon system of government.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup> The practice of slavery declined in the years after the conquest, as the Normans considered the practice backward and contrary to the teachings of the church.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup> The more prosperous peasants, however, lost influence and power as the Normans made holding land more dependent on providing labour services to the local lord.<sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup> They sank down the economic hierarchy, swelling the numbers of unfree <a href="/wiki/Villein" title="Villein">villeins</a> or <a href="/wiki/Serfs" class="mw-redirect" title="Serfs">serfs</a>, forbidden to leave their manor or seek alternative employment.<sup id="cite_ref-DouglasP312_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DouglasP312-103">&#91;100&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>At the centre of power, the kings employed a succession of clergy as <a href="/wiki/Lord_Chancellor" title="Lord Chancellor">chancellors</a>, responsible for running the royal chancery, while the <i><a href="/wiki/Familia_regis" class="mw-redirect" title="Familia regis">familia regis</a></i>, the military household, emerged to act as a bodyguard and military staff.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">&#91;101&#93;</a></sup> England\'s bishops continued to form an important part in local administration, alongside the nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105">&#91;102&#93;</a></sup> Henry I and Henry II both implemented significant <a href="/wiki/Legal_reform" class="mw-redirect" title="Legal reform">legal reforms</a>, extending and widening the scope of centralised, royal law; by the 1180s, the basis for the future <a href="/wiki/English_law" title="English law">English</a> <a href="/wiki/Common_law" title="Common law">common law</a> had largely been established, with a standing law court in Westminster\xe2\x80\x94an early <a href="/wiki/Common_Bench" class="mw-redirect" title="Common Bench">Common Bench</a>\xe2\x80\x94and travelling judges conducting <a href="/wiki/Eyre_(legal_term)" title="Eyre (legal term)">eyres</a> around the country. King John extended the royal role in delivering justice, and the extent of appropriate royal intervention was one of the issues addressed in the <i>Magna Carta</i> of 1215.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106">&#91;103&#93;</a></sup> The emerging legal system reinvigorated the institution of serfdom in the 13th century by drawing an increasingly sharp distinction between freemen and villeins.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">&#91;104&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Many tensions existed within the system of government.<sup id="cite_ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108">&#91;105&#93;</a></sup> Royal landownings and wealth stretched across England, and placed the king in a privileged position above even the most powerful of the noble elite.<sup id="cite_ref-109" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-109">&#91;106&#93;</a></sup> Successive kings, though, still needed more resources to pay for military campaigns, conduct building programmes or to reward their followers, and this meant exercising their feudal rights to interfere in the land-holdings of nobles.<sup id="cite_ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110">&#91;107&#93;</a></sup> This was contentious and a frequent issue of complaint, as there was a growing belief that land should be held by hereditary right, not through the favour of the king.<sup id="cite_ref-111" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-111">&#91;108&#93;</a></sup> Property and wealth became increasingly focused in the hands of a subset of the nobility, the great magnates, at the expense of the wider baronage, encouraging the breakdown of some aspects of local feudalism.<sup id="cite_ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112">&#91;109&#93;</a></sup> As time went by, the Norman nobility intermarried with many of the great Anglo-Saxon families, and the links with the Duchy began to weaken.<sup id="cite_ref-113" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-113">&#91;110&#93;</a></sup> By the late 12th century, mobilising the English barons to fight on the continent was proving difficult, and John\'s attempts to do so ended in civil war. Civil strife re-emerged under Henry III, with the rebel barons in 1258\xe2\x80\x9359 demanding widespread reforms, and an early version of Parliament was summoned in 1265 to represent the rebel interests.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">&#91;111&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span id="Late_Middle_Ages_.281272.E2.80.931485.29_2"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Late_Middle_Ages_(1272\xe2\x80\x931485)_2">Late Middle Ages (1272\xe2\x80\x931485)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Late Middle Ages (1272\xe2\x80\x931485)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Social_history_of_England#Late_medieval_society" class="mw-redirect" title="Social history of England">Social history of the Late Middle Ages</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_of_England_(Order_of_the_Garter).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg/220px-Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg" width="220" height="299" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg/330px-Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg/440px-Edward_III_of_England_%28Order_of_the_Garter%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="648" data-file-height="880" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Edward_III_of_England_(Order_of_the_Garter).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Early 15th-century depiction of <a href="/wiki/Edward_III_of_England" title="Edward III of England">Edward III</a>, shown wearing the <a href="/wiki/Chivalric" class="mw-redirect" title="Chivalric">chivalric</a> symbols of the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a></div></div></div>\n<p>On becoming king in 1272, Edward I reestablished royal power, overhauling the royal finances and appealing to the broader English elite by using Parliament to authorise the raising of new taxes and to hear petitions concerning abuses of local governance.<sup id="cite_ref-115" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-115">&#91;112&#93;</a></sup> This political balance collapsed under Edward II and savage civil wars broke out during the 1320s.<sup id="cite_ref-116" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-116">&#91;113&#93;</a></sup> Edward III restored order once more with the help of a majority of the nobility, exercising power through the <a href="/wiki/Exchequer" title="Exchequer">exchequer</a>, the common bench and the royal household.<sup id="cite_ref-117" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-117">&#91;114&#93;</a></sup> This government was better organised and on a larger scale than ever before, and by the 14th century the king\'s formerly peripatetic <a href="/wiki/Chancery_(medieval_office)" title="Chancery (medieval office)">chancery</a> had to take up permanent residence in <a href="/wiki/Westminster" title="Westminster">Westminster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-118" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-118">&#91;115&#93;</a></sup> Edward used Parliament even more than his predecessors to handle general administration, to legislate and to raise the necessary taxes to pay for the wars in France.<sup id="cite_ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119">&#91;116&#93;</a></sup> The royal lands\xe2\x80\x94and incomes from them\xe2\x80\x94had diminished over the years, and increasingly frequent taxation was required to support royal initiatives.<sup id="cite_ref-120" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-120">&#91;117&#93;</a></sup> Edward held elaborate chivalric events in an effort to unite his supporters around the symbols of knighthood.<sup id="cite_ref-121" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-121">&#91;118&#93;</a></sup> The ideal of <a href="/wiki/Chivalry" title="Chivalry">chivalry</a> continued to develop throughout the 14th century, reflected in the growth of <a href="/wiki/Chivalric_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Chivalric order">knightly orders</a> (including the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_the_Garter" title="Order of the Garter">Order of the Garter</a>), grand <a href="/wiki/Tournament" title="Tournament">tournaments</a> and <a href="/wiki/Round_Table_(tournament)" title="Round Table (tournament)">round table</a> events.<sup id="cite_ref-122" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-122">&#91;119&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Society and government in England in the early 14th century were challenged by the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_of_1315%E2%80%931317" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Famine of 1315\xe2\x80\x931317">Great Famine</a> and the Black Death.<sup id="cite_ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123">&#91;120&#93;</a></sup> The economic and demographic crisis created a sudden surplus of land, undermining the ability of landowners to exert their feudal rights and causing a collapse in incomes from rented lands.<sup id="cite_ref-124" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-124">&#91;121&#93;</a></sup> Wages soared, as employers competed for a scarce workforce. Legislation was introduced to <a href="/wiki/Incomes_policy" title="Incomes policy">limit wages</a> and to <a href="/wiki/Sumptuary_laws" class="mw-redirect" title="Sumptuary laws">prevent the consumption of luxury goods</a> by the lower classes, with prosecutions coming to take up most of the legal system\'s energy and time.<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">&#91;122&#93;</a></sup> A <a href="/wiki/Tax_per_head" class="mw-redirect" title="Tax per head">poll tax</a> was introduced in 1377 that spread the costs of the war in France more widely across the whole population.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">&#91;123&#93;</a></sup> The tensions spilled over into violence in the summer of 1381 in the form of the Peasants\' Revolt; a violent retribution followed, with as many as 7,000 alleged rebels executed.<sup id="cite_ref-127" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-127">&#91;124&#93;</a></sup> A new class of <a href="/wiki/Gentry" title="Gentry">gentry</a> emerged as a result of these changes, renting land from the major nobility to farm out at a profit. The legal system continued to expand during the 14th century, dealing with an ever-wider set of complex problems.<sup id="cite_ref-Fleming,_p._205_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fleming,_p._205-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the time that Richard II was deposed in 1399, the power of the major noble magnates had grown considerably; powerful rulers such as Henry IV would contain them, but during the minority of Henry VI they controlled the country.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">&#91;125&#93;</a></sup> The magnates depended upon their income from rent and trade to allow them to maintain groups of paid, armed retainers, often sporting controversial livery, and buy support amongst the wider gentry; this system has been dubbed <a href="/wiki/Bastard_feudalism" title="Bastard feudalism">bastard feudalism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129">&#91;126&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-131" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-131">&#91;nb 4&#93;</a></sup> Their influence was exerted both through the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Lords" title="House of Lords">House of Lords</a> at Parliament and through the king\'s council.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">&#91;128&#93;</a></sup> The gentry and wealthier townsmen exercised increasing influence through the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Commons_of_England" title="House of Commons of England">House of Commons</a>, opposing raising taxes to pay for the French wars.<sup id="cite_ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133">&#91;129&#93;</a></sup> By the 1430s and 1440s the English government was in major financial difficulties, leading to the crisis of 1450 and a popular revolt under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Jack_Cade" class="mw-redirect" title="Jack Cade">Jack Cade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134">&#91;130&#93;</a></sup> Law and order deteriorated, and the crown was unable to intervene in the factional fighting between different nobles and their followers.<sup id="cite_ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135">&#91;131&#93;</a></sup> The resulting Wars of the Roses saw a savage escalation of violence between the noble leaderships of both sides: captured enemies were executed and family lands <a href="/wiki/Attainted" class="mw-redirect" title="Attainted">attainted</a>. By the time that Henry VII took the throne in 1485, England\'s governmental and social structures had been substantially weakened, with whole noble lines extinguished.<sup id="cite_ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136">&#91;132&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Women_in_society">Women in society</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Women in society">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Women_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Women in the Middle Ages">Women in the Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_women" class="mw-redirect" title="Anglo-Saxon women">Anglo-Saxon women</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg/180px-Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg" width="180" height="280" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg/270px-Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/76/Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg 2x" data-file-width="325" data-file-height="506" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Hunterian_Psalter_c._1170_Eve_spinning.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A depiction of an English woman c. 1170 using a <a href="/wiki/Spindle_(textiles)" title="Spindle (textiles)">spindle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Distaff" title="Distaff">distaff</a>, while caring for a young child</div></div></div>\n<p>Medieval England was a <a href="/wiki/Patriarchy" title="Patriarchy">patriarchal society</a> and the lives of women were heavily influenced by contemporary beliefs about gender and authority.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">&#91;133&#93;</a></sup> However, the position of women varied considerably according to various factors, including their <a href="/wiki/Social_class" title="Social class">social class</a>; whether they were unmarried, married, widowed or remarried; and in which part of the country they lived.<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">&#91;134&#93;</a></sup> Significant gender inequities persisted throughout the period, as women typically had more limited life-choices, access to employment and trade, and legal rights than men.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">&#91;135&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In Anglo-Saxon society, noblewomen enjoyed considerable rights and status, although the society was still firmly patriarchal.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">&#91;136&#93;</a></sup> Some exercised power as abbesses, exerting widespread influence across the early English Church, although their wealth and authority diminished with the monastic reforms of the 9th century.<sup id="cite_ref-141" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-141">&#91;137&#93;</a></sup> Anglo-Saxon queens began to hold lands in their own right in the 10th century and their households contributed to the running of the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142">&#91;138&#93;</a></sup> Although women could not lead military forces, in the absence of their husbands some noblewomen led the defence of manors and towns.<sup id="cite_ref-143" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-143">&#91;139&#93;</a></sup> Most Anglo-Saxon women, however, worked on the land as part of the agricultural community, or as <a href="/wiki/Brewer" class="mw-redirect" title="Brewer">brewers</a> or <a href="/wiki/Baker" title="Baker">bakers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-144" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-144">&#91;140&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>After the Norman invasion, the position of women in society changed. The rights and roles of women became more sharply defined, in part as a result of the development of the feudal system and the expansion of the English legal system; some women benefited from this, while others lost out.<sup id="cite_ref-145" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-145">&#91;141&#93;</a></sup> The rights of widows were formally laid down in law by the end of the 12th century, clarifying the right of free women to own property, but this did not necessarily prevent women from being <a href="/wiki/Forced_marriage" title="Forced marriage">forcibly</a> remarried against their wishes.<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">&#91;142&#93;</a></sup> The growth of governmental institutions under a succession of bishops reduced the role of queens and their households in formal government. Married or widowed noblewomen remained significant cultural and religious patrons and played an important part in political and military events, even if chroniclers were uncertain if this was appropriate behaviour.<sup id="cite_ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147">&#91;143&#93;</a></sup> As in earlier centuries, most women worked in agriculture, but here roles became more clearly gendered, with <a href="/wiki/Ploughing" class="mw-redirect" title="Ploughing">ploughing</a> and managing the fields defined as men\'s work, for example, and <a href="/wiki/Dairy_farming" title="Dairy farming">dairy production</a> becoming dominated by women.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">&#91;144&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The years after the Black Death left many women widows; in the wider economy labour was in short supply and land was suddenly readily available.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">&#91;145&#93;</a></sup> In rural areas peasant women could enjoy a better standard of living than ever before, but the amount of work being done by women may have increased.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">&#91;146&#93;</a></sup> Many other women travelled to the towns and cities, to the point where they outnumbered men in some settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">&#91;147&#93;</a></sup> There they worked with their husbands, or in a limited number of occupations, including <a href="/wiki/Spinning_(textiles)" title="Spinning (textiles)">spinning</a>, making clothes, <a href="/wiki/Victualling" class="mw-redirect" title="Victualling">victualling</a> and as servants.<sup id="cite_ref-152" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-152">&#91;148&#93;</a></sup> Some women became full-time <a href="/wiki/Ale" title="Ale">ale</a> brewers, until they were pushed out of business by the male-dominated <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> industry in the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-153" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-153">&#91;149&#93;</a></sup> Higher status jobs and apprenticeships, however, remained closed to women.<sup id="cite_ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154">&#91;150&#93;</a></sup> As in earlier times, noblewomen exercised power on their estates in their husbands\' absence and again, if necessary, defended them in sieges and skirmishes.<sup id="cite_ref-155" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-155">&#91;151&#93;</a></sup> Wealthy widows who could successfully claim their rightful share of their late husband\'s property could live as powerful members of the community in their own right.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">&#91;152&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Identity">Identity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Identity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/English_national_identity" title="English national identity">English national identity</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG/250px-Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG/375px-Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG/500px-Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Castell_de_Windsor_-_Capella_de_Sant_Jordi.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic</a> <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulted ceiling</a> of <a href="/wiki/St_George%27s_Chapel,_Windsor_Castle" title="St George&#39;s Chapel, Windsor Castle">St George\'s Chapel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Windsor_Castle" title="Windsor Castle">Windsor Castle</a></div></div></div>\n<p>An English cultural identity first emerged from the interaction of the Germanic immigrants of the 5th and 6th centuries and the indigenous Romano-British inhabitants.<sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">&#91;153&#93;</a></sup> Although early medieval chroniclers described the immigrants as Angles and Saxons, they came from a much wider area across Northern Europe, and represented a range of different ethnic groups.<sup id="cite_ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158">&#91;154&#93;</a></sup> Over the 6th century, however, these different groups began to coalesce into stratified societies across England, roughly corresponding to the later Angle and Saxon kingdoms recorded by <a href="/wiki/Bede" title="Bede">Bede</a> in the 8th century.<sup id="cite_ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159">&#91;155&#93;</a></sup> By the 9th century, the term the <i>Angelcynn</i> was being officially used to refer to a single English people, and promoted for propaganda purposes by chroniclers and kings to inspire resistance to the Danish invasions.<sup id="cite_ref-CarpenterP3_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarpenterP3-160">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Normans and French who arrived after the conquest saw themselves as different from the English.
