They had close family and economic links to the Duchy of Normandy, spoke <a href="/wiki/Norman_French" class="mw-redirect" title="Norman French">Norman French</a> and had their own distinctive culture.<sup id="cite_ref-CarpenterP3_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CarpenterP3-160">&#91;156&#93;</a></sup> For many years, to be English was to be associated with military failure and serfdom.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">&#91;157&#93;</a></sup> During the 12th century, the divisions between the English and Normans began to dissolve as a result of intermarriage and cohabitation.<sup id="cite_ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162">&#91;158&#93;</a></sup> By the end of the 12th century, and possibly as early as the 1150, contemporary commentators believed the two peoples to be blending, and the loss of the Duchy in 1204 reinforced this trend.<sup id="cite_ref-163" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-163">&#91;159&#93;</a></sup> The resulting society still prized wider French cultural values, however, and French remained the language of the court, business and international affairs, even if Parisians mocked the English for their poor pronunciation.<sup id="cite_ref-164" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-164">&#91;160&#93;</a></sup> By the 14th century, however, French was increasingly having to be formally taught, rather than being learnt naturally in the home, although the aristocracy would typically spend many years of their lives in France and remained entirely comfortable working in French.<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">&#91;161&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the 12th and 13th centuries, the English began to consider themselves superior to the Welsh, Scots and <a href="/wiki/Bretons" title="Bretons">Bretons</a>. The English perceived themselves as civilised, economically prosperous and properly Christian, while the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_fringe" class="mw-redirect" title="Celtic fringe">Celtic fringe</a> was considered lazy, barbarous and backward.<sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">&#91;162&#93;</a></sup> Following the invasion of Ireland in the late 12th century, similar feelings were expressed about the Irish, with the distinctions clarified and reinforced in 14th-century English legislation.<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">&#91;163&#93;</a></sup> The English also felt strongly about the foreign traders who lived in the special enclaves in London in the Late Middle Ages; the position of the Jews is described below, but Italian and Baltic traders were also regarded as aliens and were frequently the targets of violence during economic downturns.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks,_pp._52-53_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks,_pp._52-53-168">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> Even within England, different identities abounded, each with their own sense of status and importance. Regional identities could be important - men and women from <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>, for example, had a clear identity within English society, and professional groups with a distinct identity, such as lawyers, engaged in open fighting with others in cities such as London.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">&#91;165&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Jews">Jews</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Jews">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_England_(1066%E2%80%931290)" title="History of the Jews in England (1066\xe2\x80\x931290)">History of the Jews in England (1066\xe2\x80\x931290)</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photograph of a small castle on top of a green mound; the castle has three circular walls visible. Behind the castle the sky is overcast and dark grey." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg/220px-Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg" width="220" height="141" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg/330px-Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg/440px-Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="410" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Clifford%27s_Tower_-_geograph.org.uk_-_257813.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/York_Castle" title="York Castle">Clifford\'s Tower</a> in the city of <a href="/wiki/York" title="York">York</a>, the site of an anti-Jewish <a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">pogrom</a> in 1190</div></div></div>\n<p>The Jewish community played an important role in England throughout much of the period. The first Jews arrived in England in the aftermath of the Norman invasion, when William the Conqueror brought over wealthy members of the <a href="/wiki/Rouen" title="Rouen">Rouen</a> community in Normandy to settle in London.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">&#91;166&#93;</a></sup> The Jewish community expanded out across England and provided essential money-lending and banking services that were otherwise banned by the <a href="/wiki/Usury_law" class="mw-redirect" title="Usury law">usury laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">&#91;167&#93;</a></sup> During the 12th century, the Jewish financial community grew richer still, operating under royal protection and providing the king with a source of ready credit.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">&#91;168&#93;</a></sup> All major towns had Jewish centres, and even the smaller towns saw visits by travelling Jewish merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173">&#91;169&#93;</a></sup> Towards the end of Henry II\'s reign, however, the king ceased to borrow from the Jewish community and instead turned to extracting money from them through arbitrary taxation and fines.<sup id="cite_ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174">&#91;170&#93;</a></sup> The Jews became vilified and accusations were made that they conducted <a href="/wiki/Child_sacrifice" title="Child sacrifice">ritual child murder</a>, encouraging the <a href="/wiki/Pogrom" title="Pogrom">pogroms</a> carried out against Jewish communities in the reign of Richard I.<sup id="cite_ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175">&#91;171&#93;</a></sup> After an initially peaceful start to John\'s reign, the king again began to extort money from the Jewish community and, with the breakdown in order in 1215, the Jews were subject to fresh attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-176" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-176">&#91;172&#93;</a></sup> Henry&#160;III restored some protection and Jewish money-lending began to recover.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">&#91;173&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, the Jewish community became increasingly impoverished and was finally <a href="/wiki/Edict_of_Expulsion" title="Edict of Expulsion">expelled from England</a> in 1290 by Edward&#160;I, being replaced by foreign merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178">&#91;174&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Medieval_England" title="Religion in Medieval England">Religion in Medieval England</a></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rise_of_Christianity">Rise of Christianity</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Rise of Christianity">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_mission" title="Gregorian mission">Gregorian mission</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hiberno-Scottish_mission" title="Hiberno-Scottish mission">Hiberno-Scottish mission</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg/220px-BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg" width="220" height="307" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg/330px-BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg/440px-BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1160" data-file-height="1617" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BLW_Reliquary_Cross_trimmed.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anglo-Saxon <a href="/wiki/Reliquary" title="Reliquary">reliquary</a> <a href="/wiki/Christian_cross" title="Christian cross">cross</a>, with English-carved <a href="/wiki/Walrus_ivory" title="Walrus ivory">walrus ivory</a> <a href="/wiki/Christ" class="mw-redirect" title="Christ">Christ</a> and German gold and <a href="/wiki/Cedar_wood" title="Cedar wood">cedar</a> cross, c. 1000</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> had been the official imperial religion of the Roman Empire, and the first churches were built in England in the second half of the 4th century, overseen by a hierarchy of bishops and priests.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingPP121_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingPP121-179">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> Many existing <a href="/wiki/Pagan" class="mw-redirect" title="Pagan">pagan</a> shrines were converted to Christian use and few pagan sites still operated by the 5th century.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingPP121_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingPP121-179">&#91;175&#93;</a></sup> The collapse of the Roman system in the late 5th century, however, brought about the end of formal Christian religion in the east of England, and the new Germanic immigrants arrived with their own <a href="/wiki/Polytheistic" class="mw-redirect" title="Polytheistic">polytheistic</a> gods, including <a href="/wiki/Woden" class="mw-redirect" title="Woden">Woden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thunor" class="mw-redirect" title="Thunor">Thunor</a> and <a href="/wiki/T%C3%BDr" title="T\xc3\xbdr">Tiw</a>, still reflected in various English place names.<sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">&#91;176&#93;</a></sup> Despite the resurgence of paganism in England, Christian communities still survived in more western areas such as <a href="/wiki/Gloucestershire" title="Gloucestershire">Gloucestershire</a> and <a href="/wiki/Somerset" title="Somerset">Somerset</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-181" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-181">&#91;177&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The movement towards Christianity began again in the late 6th and 7th centuries, helped by the conversion of the <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Franks</a> in Northern France, who carried considerable influence in England.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingP152_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingP152-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> Pope <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_I" title="Pope Gregory I">Gregory I</a> sent a team of missionaries to convert King <a href="/wiki/%C3%86thelberht_of_Kent" title="\xc3\x86thelberht of Kent">\xc3\x86thelberht of Kent</a> and his household, starting the process of converting Kent.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingP152_182-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingP152-182">&#91;178&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine</a> became the first <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_Canterbury" title="Archbishop of Canterbury">Archbishop of Canterbury</a> and started to build new churches across the South-East, reusing existing pagan shrines.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">&#91;179&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Oswald_of_Northumbria" title="Oswald of Northumbria">Oswald</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oswiu_of_Northumbria" class="mw-redirect" title="Oswiu of Northumbria">Oswiu</a>, kings of Northumbria, were converted in the 630s and 640s, and the wave of change carried on through the middle of the 7th century across the kingdoms of Mercia, the South Saxons and the <a href="/wiki/Isle_of_Wight" title="Isle of Wight">Isle of Wight</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">&#91;180&#93;</a></sup> The process was largely complete by the end of the 7th century, but left a confusing and disparate array of local practices and religious ceremonies.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">&#91;181&#93;</a></sup> This new Christianity reflected the existing military culture of the Anglo-Saxons: as kings began to convert in the 6th and 7th centuries, conversion began to be used as a justification for war against the remaining pagan kingdoms, for example, while Christian saints were imbued with martial properties.<sup id="cite_ref-186" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-186">&#91;182&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Viking invasions of the 8th and 9th centuries reintroduced paganism to North-East England, leading in turn to another wave of conversion. <a href="/wiki/Norse_religion" class="mw-redirect" title="Norse religion">Indigenous Scandinavian beliefs</a> were very similar to other Germanic groups, with a pantheon of gods including <a href="/wiki/Odin" title="Odin">Odin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ullr" title="Ullr">Ullr</a>, combined with a belief in a final, apocalyptic battle called <a href="/wiki/Ragnarok" class="mw-redirect" title="Ragnarok">Ragnarok</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-187" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-187">&#91;183&#93;</a></sup> The Norse settlers in England were converted relatively quickly, assimilating their beliefs into Christianity in the decades following the occupation of York, which the <a href="/wiki/Archbishop_of_York" title="Archbishop of York">Archbishop</a> had survived. The process was largely complete by the early 10th century and enabled England\'s leading Churchmen to negotiate with the warlords.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">&#91;184&#93;</a></sup> As the Norse in mainland Scandinavia <a href="/wiki/Christianization_of_Scandinavia" title="Christianization of Scandinavia">started to convert</a>, many mainland rulers recruited missionaries from England to assist in the process.<sup id="cite_ref-189" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-189">&#91;185&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_institutions">Religious institutions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Religious institutions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photograph of a ruined abbey; a river passes by in the lower left hand of the picture, overhung with dark trees. A ruined abbey building in stone makes up the midground of the right side of the photograph." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg/240px-Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg" width="240" height="180" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg/360px-Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg/480px-Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fountains_Abbey_-_geograph.org.uk_-_85950.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fountains_Abbey" title="Fountains Abbey">Fountains Abbey</a>, one of the new <a href="/wiki/Cistercians" title="Cistercians">Cistercian</a> monasteries built in the 12th century</div></div></div>\n<p>With the conversion of much of England in the 6th and 7th centuries, there was an explosion of local church building.<sup id="cite_ref-190" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-190">&#91;186&#93;</a></sup> English monasteries formed the main basis for the church, however, and were often sponsored by local rulers, taking various forms, including mixed communities headed by <a href="/wiki/Abbess" title="Abbess">abbesses</a>, bishop-led communities of <a href="/wiki/Monk" title="Monk">monks</a>, and others formed around married <a href="/wiki/Priest" title="Priest">priests</a> and their families.<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">&#91;187&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cathedral" title="Cathedral">Cathedrals</a> were constructed, staffed either with <a href="/wiki/Secular_canon" class="mw-redirect" title="Secular canon">secular canons</a> in the European tradition or, uniquely to England, <a href="/wiki/Chapter_(religion)" title="Chapter (religion)">chapters</a> of monks.<sup id="cite_ref-192" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-192">&#91;188&#93;</a></sup> These institutions were badly affected in the 9th century by Viking raids and predatory annexations by the nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingPP318_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingPP318-193">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> By the start of the 10th century, monastic lands, financial resources and the quality of monasteries\' religious work had been much diminished.<sup id="cite_ref-FlemingPP318_193-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FlemingPP318-193">&#91;189&#93;</a></sup> Reforms followed under the kings of Wessex who promoted the <a href="/wiki/Benedictine_rule" class="mw-redirect" title="Benedictine rule">Benedictine rule</a> then popular on the Continent.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">&#91;190&#93;</a></sup> A reformed network of around 40 monastic institutions across the south and east of England, under the protection of the king, helped re-establish royal control over the reconquered Danelaw.<sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">&#91;191&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The 1066 Norman conquest brought a new set of Norman and French churchmen to power; some adopted and embraced aspects of the former Anglo-Saxon religious system, while others introduced practices from Normandy.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">&#91;192&#93;</a></sup> Extensive English lands were granted to monasteries in Normandy, allowing them to create daughter priories and monastic cells across the kingdom.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">&#91;193&#93;</a></sup> The monasteries were brought firmly into the web of feudal relations, with their holding of land linked to the provision of military support to the crown.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">&#91;194&#93;</a></sup> The Normans adopted the Anglo-Saxon model of monastic cathedral communities, and within seventy years the majority of English cathedrals were controlled by monks; every English cathedral, however, was rebuilt to some extent by the new rulers.<sup id="cite_ref-199" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-199">&#91;195&#93;</a></sup> England\'s bishops remained powerful temporal figures, and in the early 12th-century raised armies against Scottish invaders and built up extensive holdings of castles across the country.<sup id="cite_ref-200" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-200">&#91;196&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>New orders began to be introduced into England. As ties to Normandy waned, the French <a href="/wiki/Cluniac_order" class="mw-redirect" title="Cluniac order">Cluniac order</a> became fashionable and their houses were introduced in England.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">&#91;197&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Augustinians" title="Augustinians">Augustinians</a> spread quickly from the beginning of the 12th century onwards, while later in the century the <a href="/wiki/Cistercians" title="Cistercians">Cistercians</a> reached England, creating houses with a more austere interpretation of the monastic rules and building the great abbeys of <a href="/wiki/Rievaulx_Abbey" title="Rievaulx Abbey">Rievaulx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fountains_Abbey" title="Fountains Abbey">Fountains</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">&#91;198&#93;</a></sup> By 1215, there were over 600 monastic communities in England, but new endowments slowed during the 13th century, creating long-term financial problems for many institutions.<sup id="cite_ref-203" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-203">&#91;199&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Dominican_Order" title="Dominican Order">Dominican</a> and <a href="/wiki/Franciscan" class="mw-redirect" title="Franciscan">Franciscan</a> <a href="/wiki/Friar" title="Friar">friars</a> arrived in England during the 1220s, establishing 150 friaries by the end of the 13th century; these <a href="/wiki/Mendicant" title="Mendicant">mendicant</a> orders rapidly became popular, particularly in towns, and heavily influenced local preaching.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">&#91;200&#93;</a></sup> The religious <a href="/wiki/Military_order_(society)" class="mw-redirect" title="Military order (society)">military orders</a> that became popular across Europe from the 12th century onwards acquired possessions in England, including the <a href="/wiki/Templars" class="mw-redirect" title="Templars">Templars</a>, <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutons</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hospitallers" class="mw-redirect" title="Hospitallers">Hospitallers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-205" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-205">&#91;201&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Church.2C_state_and_heresy"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Church,_state_and_heresy">Church, state and heresy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Church, state and heresy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Church_and_state_in_medieval_Europe" title="Church and state in medieval Europe">Church and state in medieval Europe</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg/180px-English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg" width="180" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg/270px-English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg/360px-English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1298" data-file-height="1805" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Carrow_Psalter_-_Walters_W34_-_Reverse_Detail.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mid-13th-century depiction of the <a href="/wiki/Becket_controversy" title="Becket controversy">death of Archbishop Thomas Becket</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The Church had a close relationship with the English state throughout the Middle Ages. The bishops and major monastic leaders played an important part in national government, having key roles on the king\'s council.<sup id="cite_ref-206" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-206">&#91;202&#93;</a></sup> Bishops often oversaw towns and cities, managing local taxation and <a href="/wiki/Local_government" title="Local government">government</a>. This frequently became untenable with the Viking incursions of the 9th century, and in locations such as <a href="/wiki/Worcester" title="Worcester">Worcester</a> the local bishops came to new accommodations with the local <i>ealdormen</i>, exchanging some authority and revenue for assistance in defence.<sup id="cite_ref-207" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-207">&#91;203&#93;</a></sup> The early English church was racked with disagreement on <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church#Doctrine" title="Catholic Church">doctrine</a>, which was addressed by the <a href="/wiki/Synod_of_Whitby" title="Synod of Whitby">Synod of Whitby</a> in 664; some issues were resolved, but arguments between the archbishops of Canterbury and York as to which had primacy across Britain began shortly afterwards and continued throughout most of the medieval period.<sup id="cite_ref-208" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-208">&#91;204&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>William the Conqueror acquired the support of the Church for the invasion of England by promising ecclesiastical reform.<sup id="cite_ref-209" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-209">&#91;205&#93;</a></sup> William promoted celibacy amongst the clergy and gave ecclesiastical courts more power, but also reduced the Church\'s direct links to Rome and made it more accountable to the king.<sup id="cite_ref-210" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-210">&#91;206&#93;</a></sup> Tensions arose between these practices and the <a href="/wiki/Gregorian_Reforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Gregorian Reforms">reforming movement</a> of Pope <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Gregory VII</a>, which advocated greater autonomy from royal authority for the clergy, condemned the practice of <a href="/wiki/Simony" title="Simony">simony</a> and promoted greater influence for the papacy in church matters.<sup id="cite_ref-211" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-211">&#91;207&#93;</a></sup> Despite the bishops continuing to play a major part in royal government, tensions emerged between the kings of England and key leaders within the English Church. Kings and archbishops clashed over rights of appointment and religious policy, and successive archbishops including <a href="/wiki/Anselm_of_Canterbury" title="Anselm of Canterbury">Anselm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theobald_of_Bec" title="Theobald of Bec">Theobald of Bec</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Becket" title="Thomas Becket">Thomas Becket</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Langton" title="Stephen Langton">Stephen Langton</a> were variously forced into exile, arrested by royal knights or even killed.<sup id="cite_ref-212" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-212">&#91;208&#93;</a></sup> By the early 13th century, however, the church had largely won its argument for independence, answering almost entirely to Rome.<sup id="cite_ref-213" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-213">&#91;209&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the 1380s, several challenges emerged to the traditional teachings of the Church, resulting from the teachings of <a href="/wiki/John_Wycliffe" title="John Wycliffe">John Wycliffe</a>, a member of <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-214" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-214">&#91;210&#93;</a></sup> Wycliffe argued that <a href="/wiki/Scripture" class="mw-redirect" title="Scripture">scripture</a> was the best guide to understanding God\'s intentions, and that the superficial nature of the <a href="/wiki/Liturgy" title="Liturgy">liturgy</a>, combined with the abuses of wealth within the Church and the role of senior churchmen in government, distracted from that study.<sup id="cite_ref-215" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-215">&#91;211&#93;</a></sup> A loose movement that included many members of the gentry pursued these ideas after Wycliffe\'s death in 1384 and attempted to pass a <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Parliament" title="Act of Parliament">Parliamentary bill</a> in 1395: the movement was rapidly condemned by the authorities and was termed "<a href="/wiki/Lollardy" title="Lollardy">Lollardy</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">&#91;212&#93;</a></sup> The English bishops were charged to control and counter this trend, disrupting Lollard preachers and to enforcing the teaching of suitable sermons in local churches.<sup id="cite_ref-217" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-217">&#91;213&#93;</a></sup> By the early 15th century, combating Lollard teachings had become a key political issue, championed by Henry IV and his Lancastrian followers, who used the powers of both the church and state to combat the <a href="/wiki/Heresy" title="Heresy">heresy</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-218" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-218">&#91;214&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pilgrimages_and_Crusades">Pilgrimages and Crusades</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Pilgrimages and Crusades">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg/220px-English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg" width="220" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg/330px-English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg/440px-English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1185" data-file-height="1295" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:English_-_Pilgrimage_Flask_from_the_Shrine_of_Thomas_Becket_in_Canterbury_-_Walters_5599_-_View_B.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A <a href="/wiki/Pilgrim" title="Pilgrim">pilgrim</a>\'s flask, carried as a protective talisman, containing <a href="/wiki/Holy_water" title="Holy water">holy water</a> from the shrine of Thomas Becket in <a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Cathedral" title="Canterbury Cathedral">Canterbury Cathedral</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage" title="Pilgrimage">Pilgrimages</a> were a popular religious practice throughout the Middle Ages in England, with the tradition dating back to the Roman period.<sup id="cite_ref-219" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-219">&#91;215&#93;</a></sup> Typically pilgrims would travel short distances to a shrine or a particular church, either to do <a href="/wiki/Penance" title="Penance">penance</a> for a perceived sin, or to <a href="/wiki/Faith_healing" title="Faith healing">seek relief</a> from an illness or other condition.<sup id="cite_ref-220" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-220">&#91;216&#93;</a></sup> Some pilgrims travelled further, either to more distant sites within Britain or, in a few cases, onto the continent.<sup id="cite_ref-221" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-221">&#91;217&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>During the Anglo-Saxon period, many shrines were built on former pagan sites which became popular pilgrimage destinations, while other pilgrims visited prominent monasteries and sites of learning.<sup id="cite_ref-222" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-222">&#91;218&#93;</a></sup> Senior nobles or kings would travel to <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>, which was a popular destination from the 7th century onwards; sometimes these trips were a form of convenient political exile.<sup id="cite_ref-223" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-223">&#91;219&#93;</a></sup> Under the Normans, religious institutions with important shrines, such as <a href="/wiki/Glastonbury_Abbey" title="Glastonbury Abbey">Glastonbury</a>, Canterbury and <a href="/wiki/Winchester_Cathedral" title="Winchester Cathedral">Winchester</a>, promoted themselves as pilgrimage destinations, maximising the value of the historic miracles associated with the sites.<sup id="cite_ref-224" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-224">&#91;220&#93;</a></sup> Accumulating <a href="/wiki/Relic" title="Relic">relics</a> became an important task for ambitious institutions, as these were believed to hold curative powers and lent status to the site.<sup id="cite_ref-225" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-225">&#91;221&#93;</a></sup> Indeed, by the 12th century reports of posthumous <a href="/wiki/Miracle" title="Miracle">miracles</a> by local saints were becoming increasing common in England, adding to the attractiveness of pilgrimages to prominent relics.<sup id="cite_ref-226" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-226">&#91;222&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Participation in the <a href="/wiki/Crusades" title="Crusades">Crusades</a> was also seen as a form of pilgrimage, and indeed the same Latin word, <i>peregrinatio</i>, was sometimes applied to both activities.<sup id="cite_ref-227" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-227">&#91;223&#93;</a></sup> While English participation in the <a href="/wiki/First_Crusade" title="First Crusade">First Crusade</a> between 1095 and 1099 was limited, England played a prominent part in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Crusade" title="Second Crusade">Second</a>, <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fifth_Crusade" title="Fifth Crusade">Fifth Crusades</a> over the next two centuries, with many crusaders leaving for <a href="/wiki/The_Levant" class="mw-redirect" title="The Levant">the Levant</a> during the intervening years.<sup id="cite_ref-228" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-228">&#91;224&#93;</a></sup> The idea of undertaking a pilgrimage to <a href="/wiki/Jerusalem" title="Jerusalem">Jerusalem</a> was not new in England, however, as the idea of religiously justified warfare went back to Anglo-Saxon times.<sup id="cite_ref-229" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-229">&#91;225&#93;</a></sup> Many of those who took up the Cross to go on a Crusade never actually left, often because the individual lacked sufficient funds to undertake the journey.<sup id="cite_ref-230" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-230">&#91;226&#93;</a></sup> Raising funds to travel typically involved crusaders selling or mortgaging their lands and possessions, which affected their families and, at times, considerably affected the economy as a whole.<sup id="cite_ref-231" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-231">&#91;227&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_technology">Economy and technology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Economy and technology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Geography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Geography_of_England" title="Geography of England">Geography of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG/220px-Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG" width="220" height="334" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG/330px-Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG/440px-Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG 2x" data-file-width="674" data-file-height="1024" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Medieval_Hunting_Park.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>15th-century depiction of an English <a href="/wiki/Medieval_deer_park" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval deer park">hunting park</a></div></div></div>\n<p>England had a diverse geography in the medieval period, from the <a href="/wiki/Fenlands" class="mw-redirect" title="Fenlands">Fenlands</a> of <a href="/wiki/East_Anglia" title="East Anglia">East Anglia</a> or the heavily wooded <a href="/wiki/Weald" title="Weald">Weald</a>, through to the upland <a href="/wiki/Moorland" title="Moorland">moors</a> of <a href="/wiki/Yorkshire" title="Yorkshire">Yorkshire</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-232" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-232">&#91;228&#93;</a></sup> Despite this, medieval England broadly formed two zones, roughly divided by the rivers <a href="/wiki/River_Exe" title="River Exe">Exe</a> and <a href="/wiki/River_Tees" title="River Tees">Tees</a>: the south and east of England had lighter, richer soils, able to support both <a href="/wiki/Arable_land" title="Arable land">arable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pastoralism" title="Pastoralism">pastoral</a> agriculture, while the poorer soils and colder climate of the north and west produced a predominantly pastoral economy.<sup id="cite_ref-233" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-233">&#91;229&#93;</a></sup> Slightly more land was covered by trees than in the 20th century, and <a href="/wiki/Brown_bear" title="Brown bear">bears</a>, <a href="/wiki/European_beaver" class="mw-redirect" title="European beaver">beavers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gray_wolf" class="mw-redirect" title="Gray wolf">wolves</a> lived wild in England, bears being hunted to extinction by the 11th century and beavers by the 12th.<sup id="cite_ref-Dyer2009P13_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dyer2009P13-234">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Of the 10,000 miles of roads that had been built by the Romans, many remained in use and four were of particular strategic importance\xe2\x80\x94the <a href="/wiki/Icknield_Way" title="Icknield Way">Icknield Way</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Fosse_Way" title="Fosse Way">Fosse Way</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ermine_Street" title="Ermine Street">Ermine Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Watling_Street" title="Watling Street">Watling Street</a>\xe2\x80\x94which criss-crossed the entire country.<sup id="cite_ref-235" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-235">&#91;231&#93;</a></sup> The road system was adequate for the needs of the period, although it was significantly cheaper to transport goods by water.<sup id="cite_ref-236" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-236">&#91;232&#93;</a></sup> The major river networks formed key transport routes, while many English towns formed navigable <a href="/wiki/Inland_port" title="Inland port">inland ports</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-237" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-237">&#91;233&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For much of the Middle Ages, England\'s climate differed from that in the 21st century. Between the 9th and 13th centuries England went through the <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Warm_Period" title="Medieval Warm Period">Medieval Warm Period</a>, a prolonged period of warmer temperatures; in the early 13th century, for example, summers were around 1&#160;\xc2\xb0C warmer than today and the climate was slightly drier.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238">&#91;234&#93;</a></sup> These warmer temperatures allowed poorer land to be brought into cultivation and for <a href="/wiki/Grapevine" class="mw-redirect" title="Grapevine">grapevines</a> to be cultivated relatively far north.<sup id="cite_ref-239" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-239">&#91;235&#93;</a></sup> The Warm Period was followed by several centuries of much cooler temperatures, termed the <a href="/wiki/Little_Ice_Age" title="Little Ice Age">Little Ice Age</a>; by the 14th century spring temperatures had dropped considerably, reaching their coldest in the 1340s and 1350s.<sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240">&#91;236&#93;</a></sup> This cold end to the Middle Ages significantly affected English agriculture and living conditions.<sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241">&#91;237&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Even at the start of the Middle Ages the English landscape had been shaped by human occupation over many centuries.<sup id="cite_ref-Dyer2009P13_234-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dyer2009P13-234">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Much woodland was new, the result of fields being reclaimed by brush after the collapse of the Roman Empire.<sup id="cite_ref-Dyer2009P13_234-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dyer2009P13-234">&#91;230&#93;</a></sup> Human intervention had established <a href="/wiki/Wood_pasture" class="mw-redirect" title="Wood pasture">wood pastures</a>, an ancient system for managing woods and animals, and <a href="/wiki/Coppicing" title="Coppicing">coppicing</a>, a more intensive approach to managing woodlands.<sup id="cite_ref-242" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-242">&#91;238&#93;</a></sup> Other agricultural lands included arable fields and pastorage, while in some parts of the country, such as the South-West, waste moorland remained testament to earlier <a href="/wiki/Over-farming" class="mw-redirect" title="Over-farming">over-farming</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a>. England\'s environment continued to be shaped throughout the period, through the building of <a href="/wiki/Ditch" title="Ditch">dykes</a> to drain marshes, tree clearance and the large-scale extraction of <a href="/wiki/Peat" title="Peat">peat</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-243" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-243">&#91;239&#93;</a></sup> Managed <a href="/wiki/Medieval_deer_park" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval deer park">parks</a> for hunting game, including deer and boars, were built as status symbols by the nobility from the 12th century onwards, but earlier versions of parks, such as <a href="/wiki/Medieval_deer_park#History" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval deer park">hays</a>, may have originated as early as the 7th century.<sup id="cite_ref-244" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-244">&#91;240&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economy_and_demographics">Economy and demographics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Economy and demographics">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_England_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economy of England in the Middle Ages">Economy of England in the Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demography_of_England" title="Demography of England">Demography of England</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg/250px-Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg" width="250" height="187" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg/375px-Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg/500px-Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2592" data-file-height="1936" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Southampton_Medieval_Merchants_House_Hall.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The central hall of a <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Merchant%27s_House" title="Medieval Merchant&#39;s House">restored 13th-century house</a>, originally built with the profits from European trade</div></div></div>\n<p>The English economy was fundamentally <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_English_Agriculture_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Economics of English Agriculture in the Middle Ages">agricultural</a>, depending on growing crops such as <a href="/wiki/Wheat" title="Wheat">wheat</a>, <a href="/wiki/Barley" title="Barley">barley</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oats" class="mw-redirect" title="Oats">oats</a> on an <a href="/wiki/Open_field_system" class="mw-redirect" title="Open field system">open field system</a>, and husbanding <a href="/wiki/Sheep" title="Sheep">sheep</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pig" title="Pig">pigs</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-245" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-245">&#91;241&#93;</a></sup> In the late Anglo-Saxon period many peasants moved away from living in isolated hamlets and instead came together to form larger villages engaged in arable cultivation.<sup id="cite_ref-246" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-246">&#91;242&#93;</a></sup> Agricultural land became typically organised around <a href="/wiki/Manor" title="Manor">manors</a>, and was divided between some fields that the landowner would manage directly, called <a href="/wiki/Demesne" title="Demesne">demesne</a> land, and the majority of the fields that would be cultivated by local peasants.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett,_p._313_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett,_p._313-247">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> These peasants would pay rent to the landowner either through agricultural labour on the lord\'s demesne fields or through rent in the form of cash and produce.<sup id="cite_ref-Bartlett,_p._313_247-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bartlett,_p._313-247">&#91;243&#93;</a></sup> By the 11th century, a <a href="/wiki/Market_economy" title="Market economy">market economy</a> was flourishing across much of England, while the eastern and southern towns were heavily involved in international trade.<sup id="cite_ref-248" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-248">&#91;244&#93;</a></sup> Around 6,000 <a href="/wiki/Watermill" title="Watermill">watermills</a> were built to grind flour, freeing up labour for other more productive agricultural tasks.<sup id="cite_ref-249" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-249">&#91;245&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although the Norman invasion caused some damage as soldiers looted the countryside and land was confiscated for castle building, the English economy was not greatly affected.<sup id="cite_ref-250" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-250">&#91;246&#93;</a></sup> Taxes were increased, however, and the Normans established extensive <a href="/wiki/Forest" title="Forest">forests</a> that were exploited for their natural resources and protected by <a href="/wiki/Royal_forest#Forest_law" title="Royal forest">royal laws</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-251" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-251">&#91;247&#93;</a></sup> The next two centuries saw huge growth in the English economy, driven in part by the increase in the population from around 1.5&#160;million in 1086 to between 4&#160;and 5&#160;million in 1300.<sup id="cite_ref-Cantor_1982a,_p._18_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cantor_1982a,_p._18-252">&#91;248&#93;</a></sup> More land, much of it at the expense of the royal forests, was brought into production to feed the growing population and to produce <a href="/wiki/Wool" title="Wool">wool</a> for export to Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-BaileyBartlettCantor_253-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BaileyBartlettCantor-253">&#91;249&#93;</a></sup> Many hundreds of new towns, some of them <a href="/wiki/Planned_community" title="Planned community">planned communities</a>, were built across England, supporting the creation of <a href="/wiki/Guild" title="Guild">guilds</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charter_fair" title="Charter fair">charter fairs</a> and other medieval institutions which governed the growing trade.<sup id="cite_ref-254" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-254">&#91;250&#93;</a></sup> Jewish financiers played a significant role in funding the growing economy, along with the new Cistercian and Augustinian religious orders that emerged as major players in the wool trade of the north.<sup id="cite_ref-255" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-255">&#91;251&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Economics_of_English_Mining_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="Economics of English Mining in the Middle Ages">Mining</a> increased in England, with a <a href="/wiki/Silver" title="Silver">silver</a> boom in the 12th century helping to fuel the <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_English_penny_(1154%E2%80%931485)" title="History of the English penny (1154\xe2\x80\x931485)">expansion of the money supply</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-256" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-256">&#91;252&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Economic growth began to falter at the end of the 13th century, owing to a combination of <a href="/wiki/Human_overpopulation" title="Human overpopulation">overpopulation</a>, land shortages and <a href="/wiki/Land_degradation" title="Land degradation">depleted soils</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan-257">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> The Great Famine shook the English economy severely and population growth ceased; the first outbreak of the Black Death in 1348 then killed around half the English population.<sup id="cite_ref-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan_257-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JordanBaileyAberthCantorJordan-257">&#91;253&#93;</a></sup> The agricultural sector shrank rapidly, with higher wages, lower prices and diminishing profits leading to the final demise of the old demesne system and the advent of the modern farming system centring on the charging of cash rents for lands.<sup id="cite_ref-258" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-258">&#91;254&#93;</a></sup> As returns on land fell, many estates, and in some cases entire settlements, were simply abandoned, and nearly 1,500 <a href="/wiki/Deserted_medieval_village" class="mw-redirect" title="Deserted medieval village">villages were deserted</a> during this period.<sup id="cite_ref-HodgettP206_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HodgettP206-259">&#91;255&#93;</a></sup> A new class of gentry emerged who rented farms from the major nobility.<sup id="cite_ref-HodgettRamsayKowalesk_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HodgettRamsayKowalesk-260">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> Unsuccessful government attempts were made to regulate wages and consumption, but these largely collapsed in the decades following the Peasants\' Revolt of 1381.<sup id="cite_ref-261" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-261">&#91;257&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The English <a href="/wiki/Cloth" class="mw-redirect" title="Cloth">cloth</a> industry grew considerably at the start of the 15th century, and a new class of international English merchant emerged, typically based in London or the South-West, prospering at the expense of the older, shrinking economies of the eastern towns.<sup id="cite_ref-HodgettRamsayKowalesk_260-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HodgettRamsayKowalesk-260">&#91;256&#93;</a></sup> These new trading systems brought about the end of many of the international fairs and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Livery_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Livery Company">chartered company</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-262" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-262">&#91;258&#93;</a></sup> Fishing in the North Sea expanded into deeper waters, backed by commercial investment from major merchants.<sup id="cite_ref-263" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-263">&#91;259&#93;</a></sup> Between 1440 and 1480, however, Europe entered a recession and England suffered the <a href="/wiki/Great_Slump_(15th_century)" title="Great Slump (15th century)">Great Slump</a>: trade collapsed, driving down agricultural prices, rents and ultimately the acceptable levels of royal taxation.<sup id="cite_ref-264" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-264">&#91;260&#93;</a></sup> The resulting tensions and discontent played an important part in Jack Cade\'s popular uprising in 1450 and the subsequent Wars of the Roses.<sup id="cite_ref-Hicks,_pp._52-53_168-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hicks,_pp._52-53-168">&#91;164&#93;</a></sup> By the end of Middle Ages the economy had begun to recover and considerable improvements were being made in <a href="/wiki/Metalworking" title="Metalworking">metalworking</a> and <a href="/wiki/Shipbuilding" title="Shipbuilding">shipbuilding</a> that would shape the Early Modern economy.<sup id="cite_ref-265" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-265">&#91;261&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology_and_science">Technology and science</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Technology and science">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_Middle_Ages" class="mw-redirect" title="Science in the Middle Ages">Science in the Middle Ages</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_technology" title="Medieval technology">Medieval technology</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photograph of a sandstone carving, broken into two pieces; on the left is the front half of a donkey, in the middle a fat man with a stick and a whip whilst on the right is a stylised windmill." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg/220px-Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg/330px-Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg/440px-Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Rievaulx_Abbey_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A medieval carving from <a href="/wiki/Rievaulx_Abbey" title="Rievaulx Abbey">Rievaulx Abbey</a> showing one of the many new <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">windmills</a> established during the 13th century</div></div></div>\n<p>Technology and science in England advanced considerably during the Middle Ages, driven in part by the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Science_in_the_medieval_Islamic_world" title="Science in the medieval Islamic world">Islamic</a> thinking that reached England from the 12th century onwards.<sup id="cite_ref-266" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-266">&#91;262&#93;</a></sup> Many advances were made in scientific ideas, including the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numerals</a> and a sequence of improvements in the <a href="/wiki/Unit_of_time" title="Unit of time">units used for measuring time</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-267" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-267">&#91;263&#93;</a></sup> Clocks were first built in England in the late 13th century, and the first <a href="/wiki/Clock#History_of_clocks" title="Clock">mechanical clocks</a> were certainly being installed in cathedrals and abbeys by the 1320s.<sup id="cite_ref-268" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-268">&#91;264&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Astrology" title="Astrology">Astrology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Magic_(paranormal)" class="mw-redirect" title="Magic (paranormal)">magic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Palm_reading" class="mw-redirect" title="Palm reading">palm reading</a> were also considered important forms of knowledge in medieval England, although some doubted their reliability.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269">&#91;265&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The period produced some influential English scholars. <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a>, a philosopher and Franciscan friar, produced works on <a href="/wiki/Natural_philosophy" title="Natural philosophy">natural philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Astronomy" title="Astronomy">astronomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>; his work set out the theoretical basis for future <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">experimentation</a> in the natural sciences.<sup id="cite_ref-270" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-270">&#91;266&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_of_Ockham" title="William of Ockham">William of Ockham</a> helped to fuse Latin, Greek and Islamic writing into a general theory of logic; "<a href="/wiki/Ockham%27s_Razor" class="mw-redirect" title="Ockham&#39;s Razor">Ockham\'s Razor</a>" was one of his oft-cited conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-271" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-271">&#91;267&#93;</a></sup> English scholars since the time of Bede had believed the world was probably round, but <a href="/wiki/Johannes_de_Sacrobosco" title="Johannes de Sacrobosco">Johannes de Sacrobosco</a> estimated the circumference of the earth in the 13th century.<sup id="cite_ref-272" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-272">&#91;268&#93;</a></sup> Despite the limitations of <a href="/wiki/Medieval_medicine" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval medicine">medieval medicine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gilbertus_Anglicus" title="Gilbertus Anglicus">Gilbertus Anglicus</a> published the <i>Compendium Medicinae</i>, one of the longest medical works ever written in Latin.<sup id="cite_ref-273" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-273">&#91;269&#93;</a></sup> Prominent historical and science texts began to be translated into English for the first time in the second half of the 14th century, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Polychronicon_of_Ranulf_Higden" class="mw-redirect" title="Polychronicon of Ranulf Higden">Polychronicon</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Travels_of_Sir_John_Mandeville" class="mw-redirect" title="The Travels of Sir John Mandeville">The Travels of Sir John Mandeville</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-274" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-274">&#91;270&#93;</a></sup> The universities of Oxford and <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" class="mw-redirect" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge</a> were established during the 11th and 12th centuries, drawing on the model of the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Paris" title="University of Paris">University of Paris</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-275" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-275">&#91;271&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Technological advances proceeded in a range of areas. Watermills to grind grain had existed during most of the Anglo-Saxon period, using <a href="/wiki/Watermill#Operation" title="Watermill">horizontal mill</a> designs; from the 12th century on many more were built, eliminating the use of hand mills, with the older horizontal mills gradually supplanted by a new <a href="/wiki/Watermill#Operation" title="Watermill">vertical mill</a> design.<sup id="cite_ref-276" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-276">&#91;272&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Windmill" title="Windmill">Windmills</a> began to be built in the late 12th century and slowly became more common.<sup id="cite_ref-277" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-277">&#91;273&#93;</a></sup> Water-powered <a href="/wiki/Fulling_mill" class="mw-redirect" title="Fulling mill">fulling mills</a> and <a href="/wiki/Power_hammer" title="Power hammer">powered hammers</a> first appeared in the 12th century; water power was harnessed to assist in <a href="/wiki/Smelting" title="Smelting">smelting</a> by the 14th century, with the first <a href="/wiki/Blast_furnace" title="Blast furnace">blast furnace</a> opening in 1496.<sup id="cite_ref-278" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-278">&#91;274&#93;</a></sup> New mining methods were developed and horse-powered pumps were installed in English mines by the end of the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-279" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-279">&#91;275&#93;</a></sup> The introduction of <a href="/wiki/Hops" title="Hops">hopped</a> <a href="/wiki/Beer" title="Beer">beer</a> transformed the brewing industry in the 14th century, and new techniques were invented to better preserve fish.<sup id="cite_ref-280" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-280">&#91;276&#93;</a></sup> Glazed pottery became widespread in the 12th and 13th centuries, with <a href="/wiki/Stoneware" title="Stoneware">stoneware</a> pots largely replacing wooden plates and bowls by the 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281">&#91;277&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/William_Caxton" title="William Caxton">William Caxton</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wynkyn_de_Worde" title="Wynkyn de Worde">Wynkyn de Worde</a> began using the <a href="/wiki/Printing_press" title="Printing press">printing press</a> during the late 15th century.<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282">&#91;278&#93;</a></sup> Transport links were also improved; many road bridges were either erected or rebuilt in stone during the long economic boom of the 12th and 13th centuries. England\'s maritime trade benefited from the introduction of <a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">cog ships</a>, and many docks were improved and fitted with cranes for the first time.<sup id="cite_ref-283" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-283">&#91;279&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Warfare">Warfare</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Warfare">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Armies">Armies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Armies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_warfare" title="Medieval warfare">Medieval warfare</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg/220px-Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg" width="220" height="193" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg/330px-Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg/440px-Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg 2x" data-file-width="966" data-file-height="846" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Coventry_Sallet_HAGAM.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The 15th-century <a href="/wiki/Coventry_Sallet" title="Coventry Sallet">Coventry Sallet</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Warfare was endemic in early Anglo-Saxon England, and major conflicts still occurred approximately every generation in the later period.<sup id="cite_ref-284" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-284">&#91;280&#93;</a></sup> Groups of well-armed noblemen and their households formed the heart of these armies, supported by larger numbers of temporary troops levied from across the kingdom, called the <a href="/wiki/Fyrd" title="Fyrd">fyrd</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Bachrach,_p._76_285-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bachrach,_p._76-285">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> By the 9th century, armies of 20,000 men could be called up for campaigns, with another 28,000 men available to guard urban defences.<sup id="cite_ref-Bachrach,_p._76_285-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bachrach,_p._76-285">&#91;281&#93;</a></sup> The most common weapon was the <a href="/wiki/Spear" title="Spear">spear</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Sword" title="Sword">swords</a> used by the wealthier nobles; cavalry was probably less common than in wider Europe, but some Anglo-Saxons did fight from horseback.<sup id="cite_ref-286" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-286">&#91;282&#93;</a></sup> The Viking attacks on England in the 9th century led to developments in tactics, including the use of <a href="/wiki/Shield_wall" title="Shield wall">shield walls</a> in battle, and the Scandinavian seizure of power in the 11th century introduced housecarls, a form of elite household soldier who protected the king.<sup id="cite_ref-287" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-287">&#91;283&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Anglo-Norman warfare was characterised by <a href="/wiki/Attrition_warfare" title="Attrition warfare">attritional</a> military campaigns, in which commanders tried to raid enemy lands and seize castles in order to allow them to take control of their adversaries\' territory, ultimately winning slow but strategic victories.<sup id="cite_ref-BradburyP71_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BradburyP71-288">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> Pitched battles were occasionally fought between armies but these were considered risky engagements and usually avoided by prudent commanders.<sup id="cite_ref-BradburyP71_288-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BradburyP71-288">&#91;284&#93;</a></sup> The armies of the period comprised bodies of mounted, armoured <a href="/wiki/Knight" title="Knight">knights</a>, supported by <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BradburyP74_289-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BradburyP74-289">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crossbow" title="Crossbow">Crossbowmen</a> become more numerous in the 12th century, alongside the older <a href="/wiki/Shortbow" class="mw-redirect" title="Shortbow">shortbow</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BradburyP74_289-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BradburyP74-289">&#91;285&#93;</a></sup> At the heart of these armies was the <i>familia regis</i>, the permanent military household of the king, which was supported in war by <a href="/wiki/Feudal" class="mw-redirect" title="Feudal">feudal</a> levies, drawn up by local nobles for a limited period of service during a campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-290" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-290">&#91;286&#93;</a></sup> Mercenaries were increasingly employed, driving up the cost of warfare considerably, and adequate supplies of ready cash became essential for the success of campaigns.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291">&#91;287&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In the late 13th century Edward I expanded the <i>familia regis</i> to become a small standing army, forming the core of much larger armies up to 28,700 strong, largely comprising foot soldiers, for campaigns in Scotland and France.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292">&#91;288&#93;</a></sup> By the time of Edward III, armies were smaller in size, but the troops were typically better equipped and uniformed, and the archers carried the <a href="/wiki/Longbow" title="Longbow">longbow</a>, a potentially devastating weapon.<sup id="cite_ref-293" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-293">&#91;289&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Cannon" title="Cannon">Cannons</a> were first used by English forces at battles such as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Cr%C3%A9cy" title="Battle of Cr\xc3\xa9cy">Cr\xc3\xa9cy</a> in 1346.<sup id="cite_ref-294" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-294">&#91;290&#93;</a></sup> Soldiers began to be contracted for specific campaigns, a practice which may have hastened the development of the armies of retainers that grew up under bastard feudalism.<sup id="cite_ref-295" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-295">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> By the late 15th century, however, English armies were somewhat backward by wider European standards; the Wars of the Roses were fought by inexperienced soldiers, often with outdated weapons, allowing the European forces which intervened in the conflict to have a decisive effect on the outcomes of battles.<sup id="cite_ref-296" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-296">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Navies">Navies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Navies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_naval_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Medieval naval warfare">Medieval naval warfare</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg" class="image"><img alt="A photograph of a small wooden ship with white sail traversing an estuary; behind the ship is a wooded shoreline." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg/220px-Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg/330px-Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg/440px-Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kieler_Hansekogge_2007.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstruction of a medieval <a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">cog</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The first references to an <a href="/wiki/English_navy" class="mw-redirect" title="English navy">English navy</a> occur in 851, when chroniclers described Wessex ships defeating a Viking fleet.<sup id="cite_ref-297" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-297">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup> These early fleets were limited in size but grew in size in the 10th century, allowing the power of Wessex to be projected across the <a href="/wiki/Irish_Sea" title="Irish Sea">Irish Sea</a> and the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel" title="English Channel">English Channel</a>; Cnut\'s fleet had as many as 40 vessels, while Edward the Confessor could muster 80 ships.<sup id="cite_ref-298" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-298">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup> Some ships were manned by sailors called lithesmen and bustsecarls, probably drawn from the coastal towns, while other vessels were mobilised as part of a national levy and manned by their regular crews.<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup> Naval forces played an important role during the rest of the Middle Ages, enabling the transportation of troops and supplies, raids into hostile territory and attacks on enemy fleets.<sup id="cite_ref-300" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-300">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup> English naval power became particularly important after the loss of Normandy in 1204, which turned the English Channel from a friendly transit route into a contested and critical border region.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> English fleets in the 13th and 14th centuries typically comprised specialist vessels, such as <a href="/wiki/Galley" title="Galley">galleys</a> and large transport ships, and <a href="/wiki/Impressment" title="Impressment">pressed</a> merchant vessels conscripted into action; the latter increasingly included <a href="/wiki/Cog_(ship)" title="Cog (ship)">cogs</a>, a new form of sailing ship.<sup id="cite_ref-302" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-302">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> Battles might be fought when one fleet found another at anchor, such as the English <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Sluys" title="Battle of Sluys">victory at Sluys</a> in 1340, or in more open waters, as <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Les_Espagnols_sur_Mer" class="mw-redirect" title="Battle of Les Espagnols sur Mer">off the coast of Winchelsea</a> in 1350; raiding campaigns, such as the <a href="/wiki/English_Channel_naval_campaign_(1338%E2%80%931339)" class="mw-redirect" title="English Channel naval campaign (1338\xe2\x80\x931339)">French attacks</a> on the south of England between 1338 and 1339, could cause devastation from which some towns never fully recovered.<sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Fortifications">Fortifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Fortifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/English_castles" class="mw-redirect" title="English castles">English castles</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_town_walls_in_England_and_Wales" title="List of town walls in England and Wales">List of town walls in England and Wales</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a href="/wiki/File:RidsdalePanorma.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/RidsdalePanorma.jpg/400px-RidsdalePanorma.jpg" width="400" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/RidsdalePanorma.jpg/600px-RidsdalePanorma.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/0e/RidsdalePanorma.jpg 2x" data-file-width="737" data-file-height="329" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:RidsdalePanorma.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A reconstruction of the city of York in the 15th century, showing the <a href="/wiki/York_city_walls" title="York city walls">city walls</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Baile_Hill,_York" class="mw-redirect" title="Baile Hill, York">Old Baile</a> (left) and <a href="/wiki/York_Castle" title="York Castle">York Castle</a> (right)</div></div></div>\n<p>Many of the fortifications built by the Romans in England survived into the Middle Ages, including the walls surrounding their military forts and cities.<sup id="cite_ref-TurnerCreightonPP20_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TurnerCreightonPP20-304">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> These defences were often reused during the unstable post-Roman period.<sup id="cite_ref-TurnerCreightonPP20_304-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-TurnerCreightonPP20-304">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> The Anglo-Saxon kings undertook significant planned urban expansion in the 8th and 9th centuries, creating <i>burhs</i>, often protected with earth and wood ramparts.<sup id="cite_ref-305" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-305">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup> <i>Burh</i> walls sometimes utilised older Roman fortifications, both for practical reasons and to bolster their owners\' reputations through the symbolism of former Roman power.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Although a small number of castles had been built in England during the 1050s, after the conquest the Normans began to build timber <a href="/wiki/Motte_and_bailey" class="mw-redirect" title="Motte and bailey">motte and bailey</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ringwork" title="Ringwork">ringwork</a> castles in large numbers to control their newly occupied territories.<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup> During the 12th century the Normans began to build more castles in stone, with characteristic square <a href="/wiki/Keep" title="Keep">keeps</a> that supported both military and political functions.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> Royal castles were used to control key towns and forests, whilst <a href="/wiki/Baron" title="Baron">baronial</a> castles were used by the Norman lords to control their widespread estates; a feudal system called the castle-guard was sometimes used to provide garrisons.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup> Castles and <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">sieges</a> continued to grow in military sophistication during the 12th century, and in the 13th century new defensive town walls were constructed across England.<sup id="cite_ref-310" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-310">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the 14th century, castles were combining defences with luxurious, sophisticated living arrangements and landscaped gardens and parks.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup> Early gunpowder weapons were used to defend castles by the end of the 14th century and <a href="/wiki/Gunport" class="mw-redirect" title="Gunport">gunports</a> became an essential feature for a fashionable castle.<sup id="cite_ref-312" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-312">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup> The economics of maintaining castles meant that many were left to decline or abandoned; in contrast, a small number of castles were developed by the very wealthy into palaces that hosted lavish feasts and celebrations amid elaborate architecture.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> Smaller defensible structures called tower houses emerged in the north of England to protect against the Scottish threat.<sup id="cite_ref-314"
