class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup> By the late medieval period, town walls were increasingly less military in character and more often expressions of civic pride or part of urban governance: many grand gatehouses were built in the 14th and 15th centuries for these purposes.<sup id="cite_ref-315" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-315">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arts">Arts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Arts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art">Art</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Art">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_art" title="Anglo-Saxon art">Anglo-Saxon art</a> and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_art" title="Medieval art">Medieval art</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg/220px-Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg/330px-Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg/440px-Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="681" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Sutton.Hoo.ShoulderClasp2.RobRoy.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Anglo-Saxon <a href="/wiki/Fastener" title="Fastener">shoulder clasp</a>, with <a href="/wiki/Geometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Geometric">geometric</a> designs and <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">zoomorphic</a> <a href="/wiki/Boar" class="mw-redirect" title="Boar">boars</a> on the ends</div></div></div>\n<p>Medieval England produced art in the form of paintings, carvings, books, fabrics and many functional but beautiful objects.<sup id="cite_ref-KesslerPP14_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KesslerPP14-316">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> A wide range of materials was used, including gold, glass and ivory, the art usually drawing overt attention to the materials utilised in the designs.<sup id="cite_ref-KesslerPP14_316-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KesslerPP14-316">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> Anglo-Saxon artists created carved <a href="/wiki/Ivory_carving" title="Ivory carving">ivories</a>, <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscripts</a>, embroidered cloths, crosses and stone sculpture, although relatively few of these have survived to the modern period.<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> They produced a wide range of <a href="/wiki/Metalwork" class="mw-redirect" title="Metalwork">metalwork</a>, frequently using gold and <a href="/wiki/Garnet" title="Garnet">garnets</a>, with brooches, buckles, sword hilts and <a href="/wiki/Drinking_horn" title="Drinking horn">drinking horns</a> particularly favoured designs.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> Early designs, such as those found at the <a href="/wiki/Sutton_Hoo_burial" class="mw-redirect" title="Sutton Hoo burial">Sutton Hoo burial</a>, used a <a href="/wiki/Animal_style" title="Animal style">zoomorphic</a> style, heavily influenced by German fashions, in which animal shapes were distorted into flowing shapes and positioned alongside <a href="/wiki/Geometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Geometric">geometric</a> patterns.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> From the 7th century onwards more <a href="/wiki/Naturalism_(arts)" class="mw-redirect" title="Naturalism (arts)">naturalistic</a> designs became popular, showing a <a href="/wiki/Plastic_arts" title="Plastic arts">plasticity</a> of form and incorporating both animals and people into the designs.<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup> In the 10th century, <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_art" title="Carolingian art">Carolingian styles</a>, inspired by Classical imagery, began to enter from the continent, becoming widely used in the reformed Benedictine monasteries across the south and east of England.<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Norman conquest introduced northern French artistic styles, particular in illuminated manuscripts and murals, and reduced the demand for carvings.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup> In other artistic areas, including embroidery, the Anglo-Saxon influence remained evident into the 12th century, and the famous <a href="/wiki/Bayeux_Tapestry" title="Bayeux Tapestry">Bayeux Tapestry</a> is an example of older styles being reemployed under the new regime.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stained_glass" title="Stained glass">Stained glass</a> became a distinctive form of English art during this later medieval period, although the coloured glass for these works was almost entirely imported from Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> Little early stained glass in England has survived, but it typically had both an ornamental and educational function, while later works also commemorated the sponsors of the windows into the designs.<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> English <a href="/wiki/Tapestry" title="Tapestry">tapestry</a> making and embroidery in the early 14th century were of an especially high quality; works produced by nuns and London professionals were exported across Europe, becoming known as the <i><a href="/wiki/Opus_anglicanum" class="mw-redirect" title="Opus anglicanum">opus anglicanum</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup> English illuminated books, such as the <a href="/wiki/Queen_Mary_Psalter" title="Queen Mary Psalter">Queen Mary Psalter</a>, were also famous in this period, featuring rich decoration, a combination of grotesque and natural figures and rich colours.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup> The quality of illuminated art in England declined significantly in the face of competition from Flanders in the 14th century, and later English illuminated medieval pieces generally imitated Flemish styles.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span id="Literature.2C_drama_and_music"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature,_drama_and_music">Literature, drama and music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Literature, drama and music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Old_English_literature" title="Old English literature">Old English literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_literature" title="Anglo-Norman literature">Anglo-Norman literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_English_literature" title="Middle English literature">Middle English literature</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music_in_Medieval_England" title="Music in Medieval England">Music in Medieval England</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Medieval_theatre" title="Medieval theatre">Medieval theatre</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg/220px-Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg" width="220" height="322" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg/330px-Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg/440px-Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg 2x" data-file-width="590" data-file-height="864" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ellesmere_Manuscript_Knight_Portrait.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Ellesmere_Chaucer" title="Ellesmere Chaucer">Ellesmere</a> <a href="/wiki/Illuminated_manuscript" title="Illuminated manuscript">illuminated manuscript</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">the Canterbury Tales</a> by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, early 15th-century, showing <a href="/wiki/The_Knight%27s_Tale" title="The Knight&#39;s Tale">the Knight</a> (right)</div></div></div>\n<p>The Anglo-Saxons produced extensive poetry in <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a>, some of which was written down as early as the 9th century, although most surviving poems were compiled in the 10th and early 11th century.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Beowulf" title="Beowulf">Beowulf</a></i>, probably written between 650 and 750, is typical of these poems, portraying a vivid, heroic tale, ending with the protagonist\'s death at the hands of a <a href="/wiki/Dragon" title="Dragon">dragon</a>, but still showing signs of the new Christian influences in England.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> Old English was also used for academic and courtly writing from the 9th century onwards, including translations of popular foreign works, including <i><a href="/wiki/Pastoral_Care" title="Pastoral Care">The Pastoral Care</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Poetry and stories written in French were popular after the Norman conquest, and by the 12th century some works on English history began to be produced in French verse.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup> Romantic poems about tournaments and courtly love became popular in Paris and this fashion spread into England in the form of <i>lays</i>; stories about the court of <a href="/wiki/King_Arthur" title="King Arthur">King Arthur</a> were also fashionable, due in part to the interest of Henry II.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> English continued to be used on a modest scale to write local religious works and some poems in the north of England, but most major works were produced in Latin or French.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup> In the reign of Richard II there was an upsurge in the use of <a href="/wiki/Middle_English" title="Middle English">Middle English</a> in poetry, sometimes termed "Ricardian poetry", although the works still emulated French fashions.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup> The work of <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> from the 1370s onwards, however, culminating in the influential <i><a href="/wiki/Canterbury_Tales" class="mw-redirect" title="Canterbury Tales">Canterbury Tales</a></i>, was uniquely English in style.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> Major pieces of courtly poetry continued to be produced into the 15th century by Chaucher\'s disciples, and <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Malory" title="Thomas Malory">Thomas Malory</a> compiled the older Arthurian tales to produce <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Morte_d%27Arthur" title="Le Morte d&#39;Arthur">Le Morte d\'Arthur</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Music and singing were important in England during the medieval period, being used in religious ceremonies, court occasions and to accompany theatrical works.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup> Singing techniques called <a href="/wiki/Gymel" title="Gymel">gymel</a> were introduced in England in the 13th century, accompanied by instruments such as the <a href="/wiki/Guitar" title="Guitar">guitar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harp" title="Harp">harp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pipe_(instrument)" title="Pipe (instrument)">pipes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organ</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;335&#93;</a></sup> Henry IV sponsored an extensive range of music in England, while his son Henry V brought back many influences from occupied France.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;336&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Carol_(music)" title="Carol (music)">Carols</a> became an important form of music in the 15th century; originally these had been a song sung during a dance with a prominent refrain \xe2\x80\x94 the 15th century form lost the dancing and introduced strong religious overtones.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;337&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ballad" title="Ballad">Ballads</a> were also popular from the late 14th century onwards, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Ballad_of_Chevy_Chase" class="mw-redirect" title="Ballad of Chevy Chase">Ballad of Chevy Chase</a></i> and others describing the activities of <a href="/wiki/Robin_Hood" title="Robin Hood">Robin Hood</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;338&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Miracle_play" class="mw-redirect" title="Miracle play">Miracle plays</a> were performed to communicate the Bible in various locations. By the late 14th century, these had been extended into vernacular mystery plays which performed annually over several days, broken up into various cycles of plays; a handful have survived into the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343">&#91;339&#93;</a></sup> Guilds competed to produce the best plays in each town and performances were often an expression of civic identity.<sup id="cite_ref-344" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-344">&#91;340&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_architecture" title="Anglo-Saxon architecture">Anglo-Saxon architecture</a> and <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">English Gothic architecture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg/220px-All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg/330px-All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg/440px-All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg 2x" data-file-width="640" data-file-height="429" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:All_Saints_Brixworth.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> <a href="/wiki/All_Saints%27_Church,_Brixworth" title="All Saints&#39; Church, Brixworth">All Saints\' Church, Brixworth</a>, late 7th\xe2\x80\x938th century</div></div></div>\n<p>In the century after the collapse of the Romano-British economy, very few substantial buildings were constructed and many <a href="/wiki/Roman_villa" title="Roman villa">villas</a> and towns were abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345">&#91;341&#93;</a></sup> New <a href="/wiki/Longhouse" title="Longhouse">long</a>- and round-houses were constructed in some settlements, while in others timber buildings were built imitating the older Roman styles.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346">&#91;342&#93;</a></sup> The Germanic immigrants constructed small rectangular buildings from wood, and occasionally grander halls.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347">&#91;343&#93;</a></sup> However, the conversion to Christianity in the 6th and 7th centuries reintroduced Italian and French <a href="/wiki/Stonemasonry" title="Stonemasonry">masons</a>, and these craftsmen built stone churches, low in height, following a narrow, rectangular plan, plastered inside and fitted with glass and colourful <a href="/wiki/Vestment" title="Vestment">vestments</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-348" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-348">&#91;344&#93;</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Romanesque_architecture" title="Romanesque architecture">Romanesque</a> style developed throughout the period, featuring characteristic circular arches.<sup id="cite_ref-349" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-349">&#91;345&#93;</a></sup> By the 10th and 11th centuries, much larger churches and monastery buildings were being built, featuring square and circular towers after the contemporary European fashion.<sup id="cite_ref-350" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-350">&#91;346&#93;</a></sup> The palaces constructed for the nobility centred on great timber halls, while manor houses began to appear in rural areas.<sup id="cite_ref-351" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-351">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Normans brought with them architectural styles from their own duchy, where austere stone churches were preferred.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup> Under the early Norman kings this style was adapted to produce large, plain cathedrals with ribbed <a href="/wiki/Vault_(architecture)" title="Vault (architecture)">vaulting</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-353" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-353">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> During the 12th century the Anglo-Norman style became richer and more ornate, with pointed arches derived from French architecture replacing the curved Romanesque designs; this style is termed <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture" title="English Gothic architecture">Early English Gothic</a> and continued, with variation, throughout the rest of the Middle Ages.<sup id="cite_ref-354" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-354">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup> In the early 14th century the <a href="/wiki/English_Gothic_architecture#Perpendicular_Gothic" title="English Gothic architecture">Perpendicular Gothic</a> style was created in England, with an emphasis on verticality, immense windows and soaring arcades.<sup id="cite_ref-355" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-355">&#91;351&#93;</a></sup> Fine timber roofs in a variety of styles, but in particular the <a href="/wiki/Hammerbeam_roof" title="Hammerbeam roof">hammerbeam</a>, were built in many English buildings.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers,_p._105_356-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers,_p._105-356">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> In the 15th century the architectural focus turned away from cathedrals and monasteries in favour of parish churches, often decorated with richly carved woodwork; in turn, these churches influenced the design of new <a href="/wiki/Chantry" title="Chantry">chantry</a> chapels for existing cathedrals.<sup id="cite_ref-357" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-357">&#91;353&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Meanwhile, domestic architecture had continued to develop, with the Normans, having first occupied the older Anglo-Saxon dwellings, rapidly beginning to build larger buildings in stone and timber.<sup id="cite_ref-EmeryP24_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EmeryP24-358">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> The elite preferred houses with large, ground-floor halls but the less wealthy constructed simpler houses with the halls on the first floor; master and servants frequently lived in the same spaces.<sup id="cite_ref-EmeryP24_358-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EmeryP24-358">&#91;354&#93;</a></sup> Wealthier town-houses were also built using stone, and incorporated business and domestic arrangements into a single functional design.<sup id="cite_ref-359" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-359">&#91;355&#93;</a></sup> By the 14th century grander houses and castles were sophisticated affairs: expensively tiled, often featuring murals and glass windows, these buildings were often designed as a set of apartments to allow greater privacy.<sup id="cite_ref-360" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-360">&#91;356&#93;</a></sup> Fashionable brick began to be used in some parts of the country, copying French tastes.<sup id="cite_ref-Myers,_p._105_356-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Myers,_p._105-356">&#91;352&#93;</a></sup> Architecture that emulated the older defensive designs remained popular.<sup id="cite_ref-361" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-361">&#91;357&#93;</a></sup> Less is known about the houses of peasants during this period, although many peasants appear to have lived in relatively substantial, timber-framed long-houses; the quality of these houses improved in the prosperous years following the Black Death, often being built by professional craftsmen.<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362">&#91;358&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Historiography">Historiography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Historiography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:203px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png/201px-Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png" width="201" height="270" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png/302px-Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png/402px-Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="807" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Domesday_Book_-_Warwickshire.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A page of <i><a href="/wiki/Domesday_Book" title="Domesday Book">Domesday Book</a></i> for <a href="/wiki/Warwickshire" title="Warwickshire">Warwickshire</a>; a key source for historians</div></div></div>\n<p>The first history of medieval England was written by Bede in the 8th century; many more accounts of contemporary and ancient history followed, usually termed <a href="/wiki/Chronicle" title="Chronicle">chronicles</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363">&#91;359&#93;</a></sup> In the 16th century, the first academic histories began to be written, typically drawing primarily on the chroniclers and interpreting them in the light of current political concerns.<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364">&#91;360&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Edward_Gibbon" title="Edward Gibbon">Edward Gibbon</a>\'s 18th-century writings were influential, presenting the medieval period as a dark age between the glories of Rome and the rebirth of civilisation in the Early Modern period.<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365">&#91;361&#93;</a></sup> Late Victorian historians continued to use the chroniclers as sources, but also deployed documents such as <i>Domesday Book</i> and <i>Magna Carta</i>, alongside newly discovered financial, legal and commercial records. They produced a <a href="/wiki/Progress_(history)" class="mw-redirect" title="Progress (history)">progressive</a> account of political and economic development in England.<sup id="cite_ref-366" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-366">&#91;362&#93;</a></sup> The growth of the <a href="/wiki/British_Empire" title="British Empire">British Empire</a> spurred interest in the various periods of English hegemony during the Middle Ages, including the Angevin Empire and the Hundred Years\' War.<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367">&#91;363&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>By the 1930s, older historical analyses were challenged by a range of <a href="/wiki/Neopositivism" class="mw-redirect" title="Neopositivism">neo-positivist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Marxist">Marxist</a> and <a href="/wiki/Econometric" class="mw-redirect" title="Econometric">econometric</a> approaches, supported by a widening body of documentary, archaeological and scientific evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368">&#91;364&#93;</a></sup> Marxist and <a href="/wiki/Neo-Marxist" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-Marxist">Neo-Marxist</a> analyses continued to be popular in the post-war years, producing seminal works on economic issues and social protests.<sup id="cite_ref-369" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-369">&#91;365&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Post-modern" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-modern">Post-modern</a> analysis became influential in the 1970s and 1980s, focusing on identity, gender, interpretation and culture. Many studies focused on particular regions or groups, drawing on new records and new scientific approaches, including <a href="/wiki/Landscape_archaeology" title="Landscape archaeology">landscape</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environmental_archaeology" title="Environmental archaeology">environmental archaeology</a>. Fresh archaeological finds, such as the <a href="/wiki/Staffordshire_Hoard" title="Staffordshire Hoard">Staffordshire Hoard</a>, continue to challenge previous interpretations, and historical studies of England in the Middle Ages have never been so diverse as in the early 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370">&#91;366&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Popular_representations">Popular representations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Popular representations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Depiction_of_the_Middle_Ages_in_popular_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="Depiction of the Middle Ages in popular culture">Depiction of the Middle Ages in popular culture</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg/220px-Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg" width="220" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg/330px-Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg/440px-Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2415" data-file-height="1833" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Battle_of_Tewkesbury_reenactment_-_pre-clash_preparation.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Historical_re-enactment" class="mw-redirect" title="Historical re-enactment">Re-enactments</a> of English medieval events, such as the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tewkesbury" title="Battle of Tewkesbury">battle of Tewkesbury</a> shown here, form part of the modern <a href="/wiki/Cultural_heritage" title="Cultural heritage">heritage industry</a></div></div></div>\n<p>The period has also been used in a wide range of popular culture. <a href="/wiki/William_Shakespeare" title="William Shakespeare">William Shakespeare</a>\'s plays on the lives of the medieval kings have proved to have had long lasting appeal, heavily influencing both popular interpretations and histories of figures such as King John and Henry V.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371">&#91;367&#93;</a></sup> Other playwrights have since taken key medieval events, such as the death of Thomas Becket, and used them to draw out contemporary themes and issues.<sup id="cite_ref-372" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-372">&#91;368&#93;</a></sup> The medieval mystery plays continue to be enacted in key English towns and cities. Film-makers have drawn extensively on the medieval period, often taking themes from Shakespeare or the Robin Hood ballads for inspiration.<sup id="cite_ref-373" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-373">&#91;369&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Historical_fiction" title="Historical fiction">Historical fiction</a> set in England during the Middle Ages remains persistently popular, with the 1980s and 1990s seeing a particular growth of historical <a href="/wiki/Detective_fiction" title="Detective fiction">detective fiction</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-374" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-374">&#91;370&#93;</a></sup> The period has also inspired fantasy writers, including <a href="/wiki/J._R._R._Tolkien" title="J. R. R. Tolkien">J. R. R. Tolkien</a>\'s stories of <a href="/wiki/Middle-earth" title="Middle-earth">Middle-earth</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-375" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-375">&#91;371&#93;</a></sup> English medieval music was revived from the 1950s, with choral and musical groups attempting to authentically reproduce the original sounds.<sup id="cite_ref-376" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-376">&#91;372&#93;</a></sup> Medieval <a href="/wiki/Living_history" title="Living history">living history</a> events were first held during the 19th and early 20th centuries, and the period has inspired a considerable community of <a href="/wiki/Historical_reenactment" title="Historical reenactment">historical re-enactors</a>, part of England\'s growing heritage industry.<sup id="cite_ref-377" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-377">&#91;373&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">At the time of the succession crisis, Matilda was married to Count <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Plantagenet,_Count_of_Anjou" title="Geoffrey Plantagenet, Count of Anjou">Geoffrey of Anjou</a>, but she still used the title of Empress from her first marriage to <a href="/wiki/Henry_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry V, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry V</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-54"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-54">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Academics have discussed the fate of Edward II at length. The majority opinion is that Edward died in 1327 at Berkeley Castle, possibly murdered; a minority opinion holds that Edward was either released or escaped, and lived on elsewhere in Europe for many years.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The term <a href="/wiki/Feudalism" title="Feudalism">feudalism</a> is controversial in current academic debate on the medieval period; depending on the definition used, feudalism may have pre-dated the Conquest instead of being imported by the Normans, and some academics consider the term unreliable altogether.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The utility of the term <a href="/wiki/Bastard_feudalism" title="Bastard feudalism">bastard feudalism</a> has been extensively discussed by historians, with many different conclusions being drawn.<sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">&#91;127&#93;</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n</ol></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=England_in_the_Middle_Ages&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 25em;
